```
docdwarf@panix.com wrote:
> In article <uJ-dnaJYC-eXqm7bnZ2dnUVZ_uevnZ2d@comcast.com>,
> LX-i  <lxi0007@netscape.net> wrote:
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
> something done by folks known as 'honorable'.

And exactly what good is democracy that fails ten seconds after we walk 
out the door?  That's irresponsible - best voiced by then Secretary of 
State Colin Powell.  He called it the Pottery Barn principle - "you 
break it, you buy it."  Democracy isn't something we can drop from an 
airlift - it has to be demonstrated, trained, and nurtured.

>> Of course - when all they hear are the Democrats saying that, after a 
>> while they begin to believe it.
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> for.

Heh - if the Jews were running it, I think we'd be just fine.  It's the 
terrorist-sympathizers that I have the problem with.  :)

>>> Not today, not tomorrow, not 50 years from now which is
>>> how long we'll be there if current policies prevail.  There must be a
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> Democrat or a liberal Republican?

You're "seeming" this wrongly.  Establishing a democracy that will last 
is a far cry from saying "You will do (x) and (y) and (z) by a certain 
date, or we will issue very, very stern warnings to you."  I saw a chart 
that the Iraqi parliament has accomplished 8 of 17 "benchmarks" 
suggested to them, while the Democrat Congress chiding them for missing 
benchmarks has accomplished 1 of the 7 things on which its candidates 
ran leading up to November 2006.  Who's missing the target here?

>> The military is 
>> there to help the process, and give them guidance.  But, the legislation 
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> and 'give guidance', as you say they are doing.

Training and reproducing is a natural part of maintaining a standing 
military - involving those of other nations in this training is 
commonplace.  In my current job, I've met officers from several 
different countries who came to our Safety Center for training.  We're 
not building their nations, but we share lessons learned and best practices.

The State Department folks are the ones giving the guidance.

>>> Socialists in the Democratic Party?  Where do you get this stuff and
>>> how do you know they've always "hated" whose success and other stuff?
…[16 more quoted lines elided]…
> administration of the distribution of goods'.

Government has no money of its own.  To have money, it has to take that 
money from its citizens; this taking of money, taxation, being done at 
the point of a gun.  (You don't pay, you go to jail.)  Anything the 
government does with the money it seizes from its citizens, the citizen 
cannot choose to do something differently.  This falls under the second 
definition you gave above - government administration of the 
distribution of goods.

Choices are being made for Americans every day, with the money taken 
from us, and being used for who knows what.  Do I want to use my money 
to bail out an airline?  Nope.  I fly occasionally, and when I do, 
*then* they get some of my money to try to help their bottom line.  Why 
are my tax dollars funding schools?  Among my three kids, they have a 
total of one semester in a public school.  So, I've basically payed for 
everyone else's kids to go to school, and I *still* had to write a check 
to a school (when we sent them to private school) or buy textbooks 
myself (when we home-school).  Sure, it's my choice, and it's one that 
I've made - but why should I still be taxed to fund public schools?  I 
don't care about public schools!

The same thing applies to all the issues I mentioned above, and then 
some.  When the government takes *my* money to give it to someone else 
(not the aforementioned "common" things), *that's* socialism.  I'm a 
pretty charitable guy - but I can assure you that if I had all my 
charity dollars to allocate as I see fit, none would be going to Planned 
Parenthood, and probably none would go to the United Way either.  Yet, 
last time I checked, both of these organizations received some sort of 
subsidy from the Federal government.
```

```
of Printing and Engraving and the Mint.  Governments say 'this is what 
will be accepted as money', make it and insure the circulation of it... or 
at least they've seemed to do so over the past few years.

If government has no money then how, pray tell, does a currency 
devaluation work?

>To have money, it has to take that 
>money from its citizens; this taking of money, taxation, being done at 
>the point of a gun.  (You don't pay, you go to jail.)

Oooooo... guys with guns, the Libertarians (sometimes referred to as

>(not the aforementioned "common" things), *that's* socialism.

Not according to the definitions cited previously about 'collective 
ownership of the means of production' and 'goernment administration of the 
distribution of goods'.  Governments which have functioned on any 
decent-sized scale have always, to the best of my knownedge, have had the 
power to take a buck from over here and put it over there; if doing so is 
'socialism' then all governments, by that criterion, are socialist... and 
that appears to be an absurdity.

[snip]

>Yet, 
>last time I checked, both of these organizations received some sort of 
>subsidy from the Federal government.

Receiving a subsidy from the Federal Government is neither, last I looked, 
demonstrating a 'collective ownership of the means of productions' nor 
'government administration of the distribution of goods'.  Insofar as that 
is correct then the situation you've cited is, by definition, *not* 
socialism.

DD
```

```
I'd love to.  But, I'd rather the bullets be fired at those who know how

legislature.  If unguarded, it would probably take very little time to 
assassinate every governmental leader over there now.  We're back to

>> Government has no money of its own.
> 
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> devaluation work?

The government prints the money - but if printing the money were the 
solution to funding the government, why have taxation at all?  "Spend 
all you want, we'll print more!" would make a great political slogan...

>> To have money, it has to take that 
>> money from its citizens; this taking of money, taxation, being done at 
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> property, right?

Quibble over how I describe it, but it's true.  If every single American 
stopped paying taxes tomorrow, the government coffers would dry up in a 
hurry.  (Granted, it would be difficult to pull that off, since they 
collect money in so many different ways...)

>> When the government takes *my* money to give it to someone else 
>> (not the aforementioned "common" things), *that's* socialism.
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> that appears to be an absurdity.

Call it absurd if you want.  It's not USSR-level socialism, but it is a 
form.  "Government administration of the distribution of goods."  When 
they take *my* money, and distribute it to *other* people, how exactly 
would you categorize that?

Should I go across the street, take one of our neighbor's 5 cars, and 
give it to the folks two streets over who only have 2?  Of course not - 
that's not my place.  I don't know what my neighbors need with 5 cars, 
but I'll bet they could tell me.  Is it fair that they have 5 cars while 
my other neighbors have 2?  We just don't know.  Maybe one house has 4 
legal drivers, while the other only has 2.  Maybe the folks with 2 are 
saving up for a third.  It's doesn't really matter, though, because the 
point of this is that it is *not my place* to take from one and give to 
the other, just because I perceive an imbalance.

Ditto with the government and people's hard-earned wages.  Mr. Business 
Owner gets 35% of his income confiscated - that's 35% of his earnings 
that he cannot reinvest in his business, to expand it and provide more 
jobs.  Meanwhile, the money seized from Mr. Business Owner goes to fund 
unemployment checks.  If the free market were allowed to work, the 
unemployment check would be unnecessary, because business would be 
expanding at such a rate that the unemployed could get work.  The 
biggest problem with this, for a lot of people, is that no one 
*controls* it.

What was that definition again?  Something about controlling the 
distribution of goods?
```

```
docdwarf@panix.com wrote:
>

> it seems.

>>>> That's irresponsible - best voiced by then Secretary of 
>>>> State Colin Powell.  He called it the Pottery Barn principle - "you 
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…

>>>> I saw a chart 
>>>> that the Iraqi parliament has accomplished 8 of 17 "benchmarks" 
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
> you give us the inverse-barometric pressure sort, too?'

Sorry to hear that...

>>> Not according to those whose assertion that the military's job is 'to kill 
>>> people and break things'.  You want training, hire DeVry Institutes.
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> underutilised bases that can be used for this purpose.

I don't think that would be effective.  I've been there, and I've been 
to several bases, and I've yet to see a base that looks like the Middle 
East.  Training them in the environment in which they're expected to 
perform is more effective.  Plus, with the situation over there, a lot 
of it is OJT - they need security *now*.

>> I'd love to.  But, I'd rather the bullets be fired at those who know how 
>> to evade and retaliate than for them to be directed at the Iraqi 
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> mission objectives.

As old as you are, I'm surprised you're being this naive.  If I give you 
something that someone else comes and takes away the minute I turn my 
back, I haven't really helped you.

>>>> Government has no money of its own.
>>> This seems to be contradicted by the busily-humming presses of the Bureau 
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
> you really need them repeated here?

I've taken economics (recently, in fact).  What stood out to me is the 
inefficiencies (AKA "deadweight loss") caused by government interference 
in the free market, through taxation, subsidies, regulation, and price 
controls.  When people work, their earnings are given to them, for the 
most part, in money.  That money is *theirs*, not the government's.  For 
the government to be able to spend money, they have to either print it, 
or take it from their citizens.  If they print lots of it, its value 
will decrease rapidly - so, they take it from their citizens.

Through withholding taxes, most people have no clue how much they 
actually pay in taxes.  Just because they don't know it, though, doesn't 
make it any less morally wrong.

>>>> To have money, it has to take that 
>>>> money from its citizens; this taking of money, taxation, being done at 
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> I have not quibbled, I believe I have shown the argument to be wrong.

No, you seized on the word "guns" and went off on Libertarians.

>>>> When the government takes *my* money to give it to someone else 
>>>> (not the aforementioned "common" things), *that's* socialism.
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
> It does not fit any of the criteria given in the definition...

It *does* fit the criteria given in the definition.  If you don't see 
that, or refuse to admit it, then this part of the discussion is going 
nowhere.

>> "Government administration of the distribution of goods."  When 
>> they take *my* money, and distribute it to *other* people, how exactly 
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> distributed by the government.

I'm guessing that you feel the recent campaign finance reform 
restrictions are perfectly fine too, then, right?  Since "money" is all 
the governments, they don't have to give us any!  When money is part of 
your earnings, it becomes your property.  Your definition does not take 
that into account, and is that omission (and I have to believe it's 
intentional) makes that partial definition irrelevant to this 
discussion.  Personal ownership of property (whether real, intellectual, 
or monetary assets) is one of the founding principles of this country.

>> Should I go across the street, take one of our neighbor's 5 cars, and 
>> give it to the folks two streets over who only have 2?
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> too.

If it's immoral for a person to do it, then it's wrong for a group of 
people to do it as well.  The things you listed at Stately Matters are 
the things I do think the government should be doing.  But even if I 
were to raise and maintain an all-volunteer army, I wouldn't be taking 
it by force from people.
```

```
"William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.netcom.com> wrote:
> "Judson McClendon" <judmc@sunvaley0.com> wrote:
>> "Charles Hottel" <chottel@earthlink.net> wrote:
…[17 more quoted lines elided]…
> prohibitions of the Old Testmament are "removed or modified" in the New.

There is not even a hint in the Bible that the New Testament "replaces" the
Old Testament. Jesus came to fullfill the Old Testament requirements of
animal sacrifices, which were always a "picture" of how sins are forgiven,
which would be fulfilled in actuality by Christ. In fact, Jesus Himself made
that perfectly clear in the passage below:

====
(Matthew 5:17-19, NKJV - Words of Jesus)
17"Do not think that I came to destroy the Law or the Prophets. I did not
come to destroy but to fulfill. 18"For assuredly, I say to you, till heaven and
earth pass away, one jot or one tittle will by no means pass from the law till
all is fulfilled. 19"Whoever therefore breaks one of the least of these
commandments, and teaches men so, shall be called least in the kingdom of
heaven; but whoever does and teaches them, he shall be called great in the
kingdom of heaven."
====

Couldn't be plainer that that. The rules about judicial punishment were part
of the Mosaic Law. In the above passage, Jesus makes it clear that He *was
not* doing away with the Mosaic Law, and that it was permanent, as far as
this world is concerned.

> Of course, PERSONALLY, (and from my denominations point of view) one of the points of the "God initiated Apostolic succession" is 
> to allow for ONGOING modifications of "understanding" of God's Will".    So I don't fully accept all of the New Testament 
> requirements either, but that is a MUCH more controversial point of view - among those claiming to be Christians in the first 
> place.

You are correct, it is controversial. But people choose to believe many things
that are in conflict with the Bible. I believe most of the 'serious' controversies
result from three things:

1) Trusting what somebody says about what is in the Bible, rather than studying
it thoroughly ourselves and believing what the Bible says. - The Bible says that
Jesus is the only advocate between God and man (1 John 2:1), and Jesus said
that the Father would send the Holy Spirit to live within us and teach us "all
things" (John 14:25,26).

2) Believing the Bible doesn't mean what it actually says, and making anything
you don't agree with "figurative" or "symbolic" rather than literal. - Not
everything in the Bible is literal, but it is usually fairly easy to tell what is and
is not from the context. If God isn't incompetent, and the Bible is His Word
written for us, then His Word can be rightly understood by us, if we take it
for what it actually says.

3) Believing that the Bible isn't a completely reliable record of what God wants
to tell us. - If the Bible isn't completely what it claims to be (the Word of God),
then it is a lie, and has nothing to tell us about God, or truth.
```

- **From:** "Judson McClendon" <judmc@sunvaley0.com>

- **Message-ID:** `<%fPJi.53779$wN3.50044@bignews2.bellsouth.net>`
- **References:** `<s8GdnRfyUvDoOWnbnZ2dnUVZ_g-dnZ2d@comcast.com> <fd4jia$ntb$1@reader1.panix.com> <zNGdnb-Q8paiZGjbnZ2dnUVZ_uKpnZ2d@comcast.com> <fd5mc6$1b6$1@reader1.panix.com> <SbadnSDaL696DmvbnZ2dnUVZ_qiinZ2d@comcast.com> <13fd73ccb00a22c@corp.supernews.com> <ZgxJi.118847$pu2.56037@bignews1.bellsouth.net> <2vyJi.74400$TK1.9691@fe04.news.easynews.com> <9wBJi.55413$7e6.54210@bignews4.bellsouth.net> <13fdqsq33ij6m17@corp.supernews.com> <rKCJi.60703$Y7.36264@bignews3.bellsouth.net> <5loqheF941p7U1@mid.individual.net>`

```
"Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
> "Judson McClendon" <judmc@sunvaley0.com> wrote:
>> "Charles Hottel" <chottel@earthlink.net> wrote:
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
>> the world" (John 1:29).

As I said, I don't want to be debating this here, because it will inevitably
aggravate a lot of people who are hostile toward Christianity. But, since
you (as usual :-) are making understandable logical points, I'll make an
attempt to answer them, without trying (or expecting) to convince you. :-)

> Exactly. What kind of a Supreme Being would require such a terrible sacrifice? For what? (Somebody with the self-esteem we could 
> reasonably expect the Creator of the Universe to have, wouldn't want lambs or anything else sacrificed, either.)

A Supreme Being that is so holy, so absolutely perfect in His nature, that
He will not, cannot, tolerate sin (violation of His laws) to go unpunished.
Because God created the entire universe, including us, He has the right to
set the standard, and that standard is absolute perfection. When man
chose to sin against God (*all* sin is against God), God could have
simply blasted humanity out of existence. What He chose to do is to take
the punishment of our sins upon Himself in Jesus, and offer free pardon
to every human who would accept it. When God put the penalty of our
sins on Jesus, He wasn't putting them onto someone else, but onto
Himself, because Jesus is God, and man. Such a thing, that the pure and
holy God of Creation would stoop to take the penalty of a sinful and
rebellious humanity upon Himself is staggering. What would prompt God
to do such a thing? The Bible says He did it out of love for us. Each of
us can make a decision to accept or reject God's offer. He will not force
it upon us. If we refuse His offer, the consequences will be forced upon
us by a God against Whom we not only rebelled, but also rejected the
offer of the death of His Son in payment for our sins. In other words,
we sin against God, then spit in His eye when He stoops to take the
penalty of our sin upon Himself. On the other hand, if we choose to
accept God's offer in faith, then we do not have to face the penalty for
our sins, because Jesus already did it.

But God offers far more than just forgiveness for our sins. When we
accept God's offer of "salvation by grace, through faith in Jesus Christ"
we are restored to a "right relationship" with God the Creator, and
actually become children of God, even joint heirs with Jesus (Romans
8:16,17). When I first began to realized the astonishing ramifications of
what it means to "become joint heirs with Christ" I was stunned! Can
you imagine what it means to be brother and joint heir with the One who
created the universe? It means that whatever Jesus has from the Father,
we have. Jesus said He wants us to be "one" with Himself and the
Father (John 17:20,21). Nowhere does the Bible refer to angels, or any
other created being than man as being "one" with God. What an
incredible, amazing thing is the Grace of God, to those who believe!

> I never asked for such a sacrifice for my "sins" and I reject it vehemently.

God gives you that option.

> Torturing a good man to death is never going to be done in my name, thank you.

Jesus wasn't only a "good man," He is also God, the Second Person of
the Holy Trinity, the Living Word of God. The Bible says that every thing
that has ever been created was created through Him, by Him, for Him,
and that He holds everything together through the constant exercise of
His power (Colossians 1:16,17). The only reason you or I or anyone
continues to exist is because God constantly wills it, through Jesus Christ.

> Who demanded a "penalty" for our "sins"?

The God who created us and everything else.

> Who decided that Eve disobeying God was a "sin" in the first place?

The God who created her, gave her life, and told her not to do it.

> (Total autocracy, it's like the bloody Daleks... "You will OBEY...". If He didn't want the fruit tasted, why put the bloody tree 
> in the garden? What kind of "loving father" decides to test his children, knowing they are going to fail, and then PUNISHES them, 
> for what was inevitable... sorry, not my kind of God... (if I actually needed one, which I don't.)

Believe me, I do understand your position, because I have had similar
objections myself. I've put considerable thought and study into this,
and I will try to give you an answer. Apparently it is very important to
God that we believe and trust Him, no matter what circumstances may
seem to contradict what God tells us. The Bible calls this "faith," and
it is the one thing that God absolutely requires from us to be forgiven,
the one "work" that we do that enables us to be saved (John 6:29).

Why does God require this particular thing called "faith" from us?
What is there about faith that God so desires? Why would faith be
the crucial determining factor between those to be saved and those
to be sent into eternal punishment? I can't give you a definitive
answer, but here are some thoughts.

Remember, Lucifer (Satan), who is possibly the highest being God
created, not only rebelled against God, but also subverted 1/3 of the
angels (Revelation 12:4). The Bible says that God doesn't really like
to roar and make a big show. This is consistent, if Satan was able to
convince 1/3 of the angels that a rebellion could possibly succeed. If
God wanted to be an "in-your-face" God, and He created the
universe, He would certainly have no problem doing that! As it is,
people often ask "Where is God?" I think God actually desires to
have beings around Him to love and fellowship with, who have
chosen, even when it looked really doubtful from circumstances, to
simply believe Him and trust Him. I think that is one of the reasons
for the "test," as you put it; to make clear, to ourselves and the rest
of creation, whether or not we really have faith. God already knows,
of course, being omniscient. Another reason that comes to mind is
that God has attributes that His creation could never see, without the
presence of rebellion, sin, and salvation. If God just crushed Satan's
rebellion and left it at that, how could any created being know about
God's grace and mercy? Also, remember that right now we're "in the
middle of the game," we haven't seen the "end game" yet. :-)

The last book of the Bible describes what are called the "End Times,"
when God finally reveals Himself, and Jesus returns, not as a lowly
carpenter born in a stable, but as "King of Kings and Lord of Lords,"
and puts and end to all rebellion. This book is often called "The
Revelation" because it starts with the phrase "The Revelation of Jesus
Christ." Many things that have been hidden will be revealed during
that time, and many questions we have now will be answered.

>>> If you believe in and want to defend killing fine. Both you and Daniel have recently advocated bombing as a way to resolve 
>>> certain problems. Of all the bombs that have ever been dropped how many killed only the guilty?
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
> you might, but you catch my drift... you would DECIDE, using the reasoning power which you believe your God gave you.

Not sure exactly what you mean here, Pete. I support capital punishment
for things like murder, because I believe God supports it. When I "decide"
to go against what God says, then I have put myself above God, Creator
of the universe. If God is indeed Creator, then His understanding is above
ours *more* than our understanding is above an amoeba's. Infinity to 1.
At that level, the difference between an IQ of 180 and 40 is insignificant.
If we believe that (which is the issue), then on what basis do we justify
putting our thinking above God's? The Bible says that the reason men do
this (and that Satan did this) is because of pride. I struggle with pride, and
I can see in myself that this categorization is true for me.

> But when it's the Bible... Oh No, no questioning THAT... don't want to be cast into the Lake of Fire...

I don't think that God wants us to "be good" just to avoid the lake of
fire, I believe that God wants us to truly accept Him as He says He is,
to believe what He says, and to trust Him; to "have faith" in Him, so
that He and we can have everlasting, intimate fellowship. The lake of
fire is the "final solution" for those who reject God and salvation.

> Can you not see why people like me exist, Judson? I'm not a wicked, evil person. I live my life to the best of my ability and I 
> care for and try to help my fellow man. I don't do it because I'm afraid of damnation, or punishment, I do it becuase it makes 
> sense and the world is a better place of we care about each other. I totally respect your right to your beliefs, and will defend 
> to the death your right to express them (I didn't start this conversation... :-)), but I honestly believe Religion in general (and 
> that includes Cristianity) is not helping the world any more.

Believe me, I really do understand what you mean and where you're
coming from, because I've been there. Even as a Christian of many
years, my faith has been challenged to the brink of failure, again and
again. But so was Abraham's faith, and Joseph's, and Paul's and every
one of the great, Godly characters in the Bible, and they were much
better men than I. But what God is interested in from us is our faith.
We choose to place our faith in God and what He has done for us in
Jesus, or we choose not to, and this is "The Test" that determines
our fate.

I also respect your right to your beliefs. If the Son of God didn't try
to browbeat people into believing, who am I to do so? :-)
```

```
disagrees with the received wisdom. Make it provocative, you sell lots of 
copies; make it too provocative, you could end up with a Fatwah on your 
head...(I read Rushdie's "Satanic Verses" and found nothing offensive in it 
at all. (great description of people falling from an exploding aeroplane, 
over London), yet the guy has to have 24 hour protection even to this day.
```

```
On Sun, 23 Sep 2007 18:34:17 -0500, "Judson McClendon"
<judmc@sunvaley0.com> wrote:

>"Charles Hottel" <chottel@earthlink.net> wrote:
>> "Judson McClendon" <judmc@sunvaley0.com> wrote:
…[92 more quoted lines elided]…
>

Yes the Old Testament did indeed condone such acts of barbarism.  For
example:

Judges 21: 10- 24 :

"So they sent twelve thousand warriors to Jabesh-gilead with orders to
kill everyone there, including women and children.  "This is what you
are to do," they said. "Completely destroy all the males and every
woman who is not a virgin."  Among the residents of Jabesh-gilead they
found four hundred young virgins who had never slept with a man, and
they brought them to the camp at Shiloh in the land of Canaan."

Numbers 31: 7-18 :

"They attacked Midian just as the LORD had commanded Moses, and they
killed all the men.  All five of the Midianite kings ï¿½ Evi, Rekem,
Zur, Hur, and Reba ï¿½ died in the battle.  They also killed Balaam son
of Beor with the sword.  Then the Israelite army captured the
Midianite women and children and seized their cattle and flocks and
all their wealth as plunder.  They burned all the towns and villages
where the Midianites had lived.  After they had gathered the plunder
and captives, both people and animals, they brought them all to Moses
and Eleazar the priest, and to the whole community of Israel, which
was camped on the plains of Moab beside the Jordan River, across from
Jericho."

However, this was all superseded in the New Testament.  While on
earth, Jesus spoke out against murder (Matthew 5:21-26; Mark
10:17-19). We also see in the writings of Paul (Romans 1:18, 29-32;
13:8-10; Galatians 5:19-21), James (James 2:8-11; 4:1-3), Peter (1
Peter 4:15-16) and John (Revelation 9:20-21; 21:7-8; 22:14-15) that
murder is wrong.

Under the New Covenant a change occurred. Just as Christians are to no
longer execute sinners, so they should not wage carnal war, but
spiritual warfare (John 18:36; 2 Corinthians 10:1-6; Ephesians
6:10-18; 1 Timothy 1:18-20; 6:11-14; 2 Timothy 2:3-5; 4:6-8).
Christians must be peacemakers (Matthew 5:9, Romans 14:19), forgiving
those who do them harm (Ephesians 4:29-32; Colossians 3:12-14; Matthew
6:9-15; Mark 11:25-26), treating their enemies with love (Luke
6:27-36) and not seeking revenge (Romans 12:17-21; 1 Peter 3:8-12).
Hatred which is the same as murder (1 John 3:15), is unforgiving,
vengeful and hostile towards one's enemies.

Regards,


 
>> I suggest we just agree to disagree since debating this will change no ones mind and just bore the reat of the group to tears.
```

```
4. None of the above.

Ruling out the points above:

1. I have read and studied the Bible from cover to cover (as I have a number 
of other Religious and Philosophical writings), and have never required 
someone else to interpret it for me. I reject it (overall) because it 
doesn't make sense and contradicts itself all over the place. The picture it 
paints of a Supreme Being is an unlikely one (spiteful, petty, jealous, and 
small minded). I appreciate the beauty of it and there is much of value in 
it, but it is far from being a model for today's world.

2. I believe it means what it says; I just don't necessarily agree with it.

3. If there were a God, it might be. I don't believe it is a "lie" or that 
we can learn nothing from it.

Complex, isn't it? :-)

Pete.
```

```
In my view (and the view of both Judaism and Catholicism), it is not crucial 
what the Bible says. It is not even important what God said. The only thing 
that counts is what God MEANT. Often what God said is not what He meant and 
often what He meant is not what He said. Judiasm relies upon "the sages of 
the generation" and Catholics rely upon the Church for accurate meanings.

--- aside
Interestingly, in Biblical translation and interpretation, the reverse of 
Occam's Razor is the rule. Occam's Razor holds that, given two 
interpretations of events, the simpler is the best answer. Scholars find in 
dealing with translations (and copies), the more complex of two differing 
texts is probably the most accurate. Early monks often tried to simplify the 
text to make it more understandable.
--- end aside

In Judaism, the method and authority for interpretation is to be found 
within the text.
In the Catholic tradition, authority and methodology for interpretation is 
vested in the Church as an institution.
In the non-Catholoic tradition, authority is vested with the individual 
guided by the Holy Spirit.

Often these three schemas converge on a the same answer, for example, 
self-defense. Sometimes these three diverge. For example, abortion.

In the Jewish tradition, abortion is frowned upon but sometimes required.
In the Catholic tradition, abortion is always prohibited.
In the non-Catholic world, I don't know of a group that has an "official" 
position.
```

```
On Wed, 26 Sep 2007 11:58:36 -0700, Alistair <alistair@ld50macca.demon.co.uk> wrote:

>On 25 Sep, 00:12, "HeyBub" <heybubNOS...@gmail.com> wrote:
>> Charles Hottel wrote:
…[17 more quoted lines elided]…
>Nil problemo.

It's almost impossible to avoid electricity. In the old days, electricity was considered
fire,  closing or opening a switch was the same as starting or extinguishing a fire
(they're not allowed to extinguish fires either). In those days, electricity that didn't
throw visible sparks or get hot was ok. For example, incandescent bulbs were out, but LEDs
were ok.

After someone, probably a physicist, explained the fundamental difference between
electricity and fire, they changed the reason from fire to construction. Closing a switch
was building a circuit; opening a switch was deconstructing a circuit (they're not allowed
to tear things down either). After the change, LEDs and semiconductor gates were forbidden
switches. Computers, including embedded processors in security cameras and motion
detectors, are flipping switches millions of times per second. What about using a DIGITAL
hearing aid? What about the automatic door opener at the supermarket, the DIGITAL security
cameras taking your picture as you walk through the store, or the RFID scanner that
electronically frisks you on the way out? What about crosswalks that sense pedestrians
with proximity switches? What about automated toilets and sinks, which also use proximity
detectors?
```

```
>malnutrition, etc., etc.

The Amana Colony, in Iowa, kept its kids in school and set its best youth to college. Most
of them didn't return. The ones who did wanted automobiles and TVs, which could not be
home-made, had to be bought with cash. They opened a refrigerator plant to earn cash.  As
a result, they were no longer self-sufficient, they were vulnerable to ups and downs of
the national economy, like everyone else. In 1931, they went broke and ceased functioning
as a closed religious group. 

As survival strategy, the Amish and Mennonites are smart to remain as  self-sufficient as
possible, and minimize outside temptations.
```

```
On Thu, 27 Sep 2007 11:30:39 -0700, Alistair <alistair@ld50macca.demon.co.uk> wrote:

>On 27 Sep, 04:05, Robert <n...@e.mail> wrote:

>> After someone, probably a physicist, explained the fundamental difference between
>> electricity and fire, they changed the reason from fire to construction. Closing a switch
…[12 more quoted lines elided]…
>Newtons and that all motion in a gravitational field constitutes work?

That's why elevator regenerative braking systems are disabled on Shabbos -- so the work
performed by descending Jews is not used productively.

All work is not prohibited, just 39 categories, which are called melachot. One melachah
prohibits carrying anything, such as keys and watches. Another prohibits constructing;
opening an umbrella is considered constructing a temporary shelter. 

If you really want to be amused, a loophole allows forbidden acts to be performed 'by
unusual means'. For instance, pushing a button with your knuckle rather than your finger,
or writing with your non-dominant hand. The word Pythonesque comes to mind.
```

```

Judson McClendon <judmc@sunvaley0.com> wrote in message news:9wBJi.55413> >
> 2) Believing the Bible doesn't mean what it actually says, and making
anything
> you don't agree with "figurative" or "symbolic" rather than literal. - Not
> everything in the Bible is literal, but it is usually fairly easy to tell
what is and
> is not from the context. If God isn't incompetent, and the Bible is His
Word
> written for us, then His Word can be rightly understood by us, if we take
it
> for what it actually says.
>
> 3) Believing that the Bible isn't a completely reliable record of what God
wants
> to tell us. - If the Bible isn't completely what it claims to be (the Word
of God),
> then it is a lie, and has nothing to tell us about God, or truth.
> --

I find these two points rather contradictory, but I don't have to harmonize
them.

2) Judging by the number of varieties of Christianity it would seem that His
word can be rightly understood any way the reader wants to.

3) All doubts about what the Bible says or doesn't say, its reliability or
its truthfulness, can be resolved if we take it to be the words of the
prophets or disciples of God, rather than God himself.  God doesn't need a
Bible, after all; he can communicate directly with all spiritual entities
that exist and pass on his truth without possibility of error or
misinterpretation.  So if the Bible is the work of men, not God (no matter
if they were divinely inspired or not), then we expect omissions,
ambiguities, and contradictions.

Judson, you said you didn't want another religious discussion; I'd suggest
you'd have been better off not to start it at all.  You knew perfectly well
that people would take you up on your statements.

PL
```

```
SkippyPB wrote:
>
> I too am Roman Catholic.  I love talking about the Bible with Jehova's
> Witness people when they come to my door wanting me to read
> Watchtower.  They get real annoyed when I call them heathens.
>

If you really want to see 'em twitch, show them the origin of the word 
"Jehovah."

Moses didn't know the word, Jesus didn't know the word. "Jehovah" started 
out as a mistake in translation by Martin Luther when he used the consonants 
of one Hebrew word with the vowels from a completely different word to get a 
third word which, in turn, was translitered from German into English.
```

- **References:** `<13fd73ccb00a22c@corp.supernews.com> <ZgxJi.118847$pu2.56037@bignews1.bellsouth.net> <2vyJi.74400$TK1.9691@fe04.news.easynews.com> <9wBJi.55413$7e6.54210@bignews4.bellsouth.net> <2iRJi.109249$lZ7.74409@newsfe20.lga> <7VVJi.111313$tu2.30117@fe09.news.easynews.com> <jaXJi.409$P21.95@newssvr19.news.prodigy.net> <1biif31ireqcjft2mi3npf7gdan652nvvk@4ax.com> <13findoqg2jnf9c@news.supernews.com> <1190833987.270600.113680@r29g2000hsg.googlegroups.com> <mcjnf35mpdt86vsv8saidbplhsb6bu1uf7@4ax.com> <1190920184.337478.249470@g4g2000hsf.googlegroups.com>`

```
Alistair wrote:
>>
>>>> Moses didn't know the word, Jesus didn't know the word. "Jehovah"
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
> and why Martin Luther made the mistake.

(using Roman characters)
Okay. In the Torah, the first five books of the Bible, the word for God 
appears as YHWH (written Hebrew has no vowels).

When reading aloud, there is a prohibition against using God's name, so 
another word is substituted: "Adona" (means "lord" as in king or master). So 
when the observant Jew runs across YHWH, he articulates "Adonai," and this 
becomes as automatic as us thinking "etcetera" whenever we see "etc."

A group of scholarly Jews, the Masorites, fearing that Jews were losing the 
ability to study the sacred texts, devised a set of diacritical marks to 
represent the vowels of spoken Hebrew. These marks were placed above and 
below the consants of written Hebrew. Now here's where the funny part comes 
in.

Regarding the word YHWH, the Masorites placed the vowels for "Adonai" with 
the consonants - because they knew that was the word that would be spoken 
aloud.

Martin Luther, who knew Hebrew but not this trick, simply transcribed the 
word as YaHoWaH. When the word was translated into English, The German "Y" 
became "J" and the German "W" became "V."

The result is Jahovah or Jehovah.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Religian (was:Iraq

_(reply depth: 18)_

- **From:** spambait@milmac.com (Doug Miller)
- **Date:** 2007-09-28T12:27:52+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<1r6Li.757$ih1.340@nlpi068.nbdc.sbc.com>`
- **References:** `<13fd73ccb00a22c@corp.supernews.com> <ZgxJi.118847$pu2.56037@bignews1.bellsouth.net> <2vyJi.74400$TK1.9691@fe04.news.easynews.com> <9wBJi.55413$7e6.54210@bignews4.bellsouth.net> <2iRJi.109249$lZ7.74409@newsfe20.lga> <7VVJi.111313$tu2.30117@fe09.news.easynews.com> <jaXJi.409$P21.95@newssvr19.news.prodigy.net> <1biif31ireqcjft2mi3npf7gdan652nvvk@4ax.com> <13findoqg2jnf9c@news.supernews.com> <1190833987.270600.113680@r29g2000hsg.googlegroups.com> <mcjnf35mpdt86vsv8saidbplhsb6bu1uf7@4ax.com> <1190920184.337478.249470@g4g2000hsf.googlegroups.com> <13foqc6bb3rr1fe@news.supernews.com>`

```
In article <13foqc6bb3rr1fe@news.supernews.com>, "HeyBub" <heybubNOSPAM@gmail.com> wrote:

>
>Martin Luther, who knew Hebrew but not this trick, simply transcribed the 
>word as YaHoWaH. When the word was translated into English, The German "Y" 
>became "J" and the German "W" became "V."

Close, but no cigar. A German would write the [English] Y *sound* with the 
letter J, as in the German word for 'yes' -- spelled ja, and pronounced "yah". 
Y is used only rarely in German. It's called 'ipsilon' in the German alphabet, 
and it's sounded "ee" or "oo" -- but never as Y is sounded at the 
beginning of English words.

What Luther would have pronounced as yay-ho-vah, he would certainly have 
written as Jehowah. Why the German W kept its sound (changing its spelling to 
V) and the German J kept its spelling (changing its sound to Y) when the word 
migrated to English is one of life's little mysteries.
```

```
instead on doing good and treating your fellow man well. (Gautama Buddha, 
Jesus, Lao Tze, Confucius...)
```

```
I think the problem here, Judson, is that these "revelations" are really 
subjective. You accept them, so He has revealed himself progressively to 
YOU; I don't accept them, He has never made any personal contact or attempt 
to discuss this with ME, (and an omnipotent, omniscient, perfect Being would 
have no trouble doing so with every single person on the planet if He wanted 
to...), so He has not progressively revealed Himself to ME.

Therefore, I believe it is incorrect to say He has revealed Himself to "us".

If we must decipher the tangle of contradictory illogical Scriptures which 
are the only evidence we have today, and come by the "faith" so attained, 
then that's a pretty poor game (I, for one, won't play it...), when our 
mortal souls are supposedly at stake.

It's time He gave us another clue, yet, despite entreaties from both the 
faithful and the unGodly, nothing seems to be forthcoming...

For some of us, we can only draw the obvious conclusion...

Pete.
```

```
Or is it the teaching of Jesus as modified and interpreted by the 
Organization set up after His death?

Unless we were actually at the Mount and heard the sermon, who knows what 
was actually said? As the technology of the time did not admit to actual 
voice recording, and we don't have a Time Machine to take us back there, it 
has to be subject to question. We can agree that what has come to us is 
beautiful and inspiring, but we don't know how much is what Jesus actually 
said and how much has been "improved and gilded" by the Scribes.

(I love the scene in "Life of Brian" where the people at the back of the 
crowd have trouble hearing and start fighting amongst themselves... "Did He 
say the Greeks will inherit the Earth?...I knew a Greek once..." " Shut up, 
Big nose, I can't hear what He's saying." Although it is very funny, it is 
also very real.)

The best we can do is take what is useful and inspiring, whether it came 
from Jesus or not...

Is it an inner "faith" that lets us do this, or is it just plain common 
sense?

Pete.
```

```

Pete Dashwood <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote in message > >
> > I really don't understand how anybody could stay sane who truly
> > believed strangers, acquaintances, friends, and family will be
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
> be strong and punishments were harsh. Simple people figured the harsher
the
> punishment, the greater the leader.
>

It's also a possibility that they didn't have the mathematics to really
understand what "eternity" (an infinite number of years) implied.  I believe
that the Greeks (the most mathematically advanced group in the ancient
world) had "myriad" (10,000?) as the biggest named number in common use; and
while Archimedes, in the "Sand Reckoner", got up into truly gigantic
numbers, even by today's standards, his method didn't affect general usage.
If your biggest number is "myriad" and your biggest expression is "myriads
of myriads of myriads ...", even if you go on for half an hour, you have no
idea what number you're really talking about.  It would probably have been
beyond comprehension that any number you could construct is zero as compared
to infinity: therefore it wouldn't have troubled them because they wouldn't
have understood what they were saying.

PL
```

```
And, of course, some people just aren't comfortable discussing their religious

they do -- often, they attend a particular church simply because it's the one 
their parents attended.

I know a number of Catholics who are quite willing to discuss their beliefs, 
and very articulate in explaining why they believe as they do. Your experience 
here, while common, is far from universal, and should not be generalized to 
encompass all of us.
```

```
The parts of the Pentateuch you quote above were (probably... there is some 
dispute about who wrote what and when... just one credible example: 
http://www.awitness.org/contrabib/torah/latedate.html) written by Moses, 
based on the folk lore and legend of his time, and in an effort to unite and 
inspire a people suffering unendurable hardship. I have always thought it is 
incredible (as a work), but I don't believe it was inspired by God, any more 
than any idea anybody has is "inspired by God". (As I don't believe in God, 
it is easy for me to say that :-))

did not. It was natural that he would emerge as a leader, and a leader 
(especially in those times) had to be strong.

But do the mores and rules of an ancient culture  (capital punishment, "eye 
for an eye" as "justice"...) have relevance today?

I think we can do better than that. If we can't, then we have learned 
nothing in 3500 years and we aren't going anywhere, either.

Maybe it is exactly beliefs such as this, that are holding us back.

Pete.
```

```
In article <5loof8F9806oU1@mid.individual.net>, "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
>
>But do the mores and rules of an ancient culture  (capital punishment, "eye 
>for an eye" as "justice"...) have relevance today?

"An eye for an eye, a tooth for a tooth" is not a commandment to seek exact, 
identical revenge; rather, it is a *limitation*: you shall not do more than 
was done to you. In a time and place in which the norm was a *life* for an 
eye, this idea was nothing short of revolutionary.
```

```
"Doug Miller" <spambait@milmac.com> wrote in message 
news:GZNJi.294$P21.186@newssvr19.news.prodigy.net...
> In article <5loof8F9806oU1@mid.individual.net>, "Pete Dashwood" 
> <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
>
A good response, Doug. I admit I hadn't thought about it like that.

Even so, revenge (like punishment) is really pointless. Punishment does not 
deter crime; changing people's minds about crime is mch more effective. 
Nowadays, we can sentence them to community service or working for the 
victim until the grievance is worked out. Both of these approaches have 
shown some promise here, particularly making miscreants confront their 
victims and see the damage they do, not just to one person but to families 
and communities. Even people who come from backgrounds where they had 
neither of these things have been known to change their minds, especially if 
they are young and have a chance at another better choice.

I have an elderly lady friend whose house was burglarized and she was pretty 
distraught. The kid who did it was made to sit down with her and received 30 
hours of service at her house, mowing lawns, doing chores and generally 
helping with odd jobs. She told me she was pretty uncomfortable with it but 
agreed to the experiment. That was a few years ago. The kid is now married, 
has a little girl, holds down a job at a local garage and calls to see the 
old lady about once a month. They are firm friends and he thanks her for 
giving him a chance. It can work. (Not in all cases, of course, but it's 
still better than plucking out eyes and teeth...)

Pete.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: [OT] Iraq

_(reply depth: 6)_

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2007-09-24T14:53:08+12:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<5lon4oF9b4ddU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<s8GdnRfyUvDoOWnbnZ2dnUVZ_g-dnZ2d@comcast.com> <fd4jia$ntb$1@reader1.panix.com> <zNGdnb-Q8paiZGjbnZ2dnUVZ_uKpnZ2d@comcast.com> <fd5mc6$1b6$1@reader1.panix.com> <SbadnSDaL696DmvbnZ2dnUVZ_qiinZ2d@comcast.com> <13fd73ccb00a22c@corp.supernews.com>`

```

"Charles Hottel" <chottel@earthlink.net> wrote in message 
news:13fd73ccb00a22c@corp.supernews.com...
>
> "LX-i" <lxi0007@netscape.net> wrote in message 
…[38 more quoted lines elided]…
> young babies in church today.

Charlie, I think you have expressed what many people (all over the world) 
are feeling. And especially people who have to watch their children and 
grandchildren being put in harm's way for what can only be construed as 
"doubtful" politics.

We are all tired of it.

But what can you do?
Simply roll over and let the strong and ruthless trample the less strong? 
How long before the strong and ruthless are knocking on our door?

The powerful nations of the Earth do have a responsibility to the less 
powerful, and it is also in their interests to try and maintain some 
stability.

Living on a remote island in the South Pacific which has no capability to 
defend itself , should the need arise. I sometimes reflect on these things. 
I am aware that the fact I am sitting here typing this in English, rather 
than Japanese, is because a number of young Americans died at the Coral Sea.

Had the Japanese not been stopped they would have occupied Australia, and NZ 
would have been taken with a phone call. I know there would have been fierce 
resistance when occupation troops actually arrived, and some of my 
compatriots would have taken to the hills and would still be fighting to 
this day, but the majority of the population (those who are not fit fighting 
men) would have had to accept it. I know that most of the Japanese people 
alive today were not even born then, so I don't translate this into an 
anti-Japanese prejudice, but I can't help but wonder how different the world 
would be if the strong did NOT protect the weak occasionally.

I guess the point is that there are times when violence HAS to be an answer. 
(I don't like it, but I am not one to ignore reality...)

I mentioned my father here before. For a part of his life he was a 
professional boxer, and he gave me boxing gloves when I was six. He used to 
get on his knees and spar with me. By the time I was 10, he was on his feet 
and I used to enjoy our sessions very much. I'd hit him with my best shot 
and he would laugh and say: "Not like that, you couldn't knock the skin off 
a rice pudding..." Then he would show me how I COULD knock the skin off a 
rice pudding. Throughout my entire school career, I was never bullied and  I 
stopped several other kids being bullied as well. Yet I never looked for 
trouble. The Old Man's advice (and I have never forgotten it) was: "Don't 
ever fight if you can avoid it. Violence is not an answer. Walk away. BUT, 
if you absolutely have no choice, then don't stop while you can stand or 
see."

(In a professional career that spanned 5 years he was never knocked down, 
and held the NZ Middleweight title for 18 months. He never boxed under his 
family name because pugilism would have been demeaning to the Old Folks at 
Home.:-) I still have his pocket watch with the initials "FF" (his boxing 
name "Fred 'Kid' Fisher") engraved on it. My mother gave it to him in 1936.)

New Zealanders are currently deployed in Iraq and Afghanistan, and one of 
them was recently awarded the Victoria Cross  (equivalent to the CMH for 
Americans) for carrying his wounded mate several hundred yards to safety 
while under intense fire from Taliban. A quiet, unassuming Maori man who 
grew up in a rural community on the East Coast of the North Island. Others 
are rebuildng schools and providing security for the populace. Even out of 
war, some good can come, but the price is very terrible.

So what can we do about it?

I believe we are living in an ideological age. It is about ideas and 
ideologies. If someone can be so convinced of an idea that he will drive a 
truck loaded with explosives into a market place, destroying himself and 
killing and maiming innocent bystanders, then we are unlikely to change his 
mind with violence. (However, I do believe that a proper use of violence in 
this context is to prevent the truck reaching the market place...)

How do you go about changing people's minds?

When it comes to Religion, it isn't easy. The best you can hope for is to 
persuade them to tolerance of other viewpoints and that doesn't seem to be 
working very well. While I respect people's rights to their Religious views 
(as long as it doesn't harm others) I'd still like to see the world grow out 
of it... :-)  I believe it will, but it will take probably a thousand 
years...

I know (because I live in a society where it works) it IS possible for 
people to "get along", and even actually enjoy the differences in culture, 
Religion (or lack of it) and viewpoint. But it takes Education and time and 
the will to do so.

It also requires that everybody has enough to eat and a reasonable standard 
of living.

So, at the moment, the best we can do is assure the fanatics that they will 
NOT achieve their aims by violent means, and that means the sacrifices that 
we are all tired of seeing and making.

It worked in Northern Ireland. Eventually the IRA were persuaded that 
killing and maiming people was losing them more support than it gained and 
we now have a peaceful solution. It isn't perfect, but it is way better than 
it was.

If fundamental Islamics and Christians were not being indoctrinated in the 
politics of hatred, if the more moderate members of both communities could 
exercise some influence over what is being preached in mosque and church, 
that would be a start.

I don't want to see young people of any race or denomination being 
sacrificed in Iraq or Afghanistan, or anywhere else.

Like Charlie, I'm tired of it.

But until minds are changed, it will continue.

I see Education rather than weaponry as the hope for the future. When people 
can read, they can get new ideas.

Meantime, whether we like it or not, we need weaponry.

Pete.












>
```

```
Just because the government creates and distributes the "money" does not

you they *won't* say "Well, nothing, since the government owns all the 
money."  Money is used as a standardized means of exchanging what one 
has for what one wants - AKA commerce.  When the government tinkers with 
the free market, the free market loses.

sum of money, where does the government get the right come to me and say 
"Well, I know you agreed to (x), but you're only getting (x-y) because 
we think we know better how to spend that money than you do."?  Ditto 
for whatever economic hocus-pocus they play on the employer.

Your focus is on what's "legal" - mine is what's "right."  Those are 
often not the same.
```

```
Perhaps, Mr Dwarf, you might prefer Mr Summers choose
words and definitions such as these from
< www.dictionary.com >.
-----
welfarism: the set of attitudes and policies characterizing
    or tending toward the establishment of a welfare state.

welfare state: a state in which the welfare of the people
    in such matters as social security, health and education,
    housing, and working conditions is the responsibility
    of the government.
-----

Also given are these additional definitions from the
American Heritage New Dictionary of Cultural Literacy,
Third Edition.
-----
welfare state

A state or government that promotes public welfare through
programs of public health, pensions, unemployment
compensation, public housing, and the like. The expression
welfare state is often used by those hostile to government
intervention in these areas.

[Chapter:] World Politics

And

welfare state

An economic system that combines features of capitalism
and socialism by retaining private ownership while the
government enacts broad programs of social welfare,
such as pensions and public housing.

[Chapter:] Business and Economics
-----

These two additional definitions, particularly the ideas
"often used by those hostile to government intervention
in these areas" and "features of ... socialism", seem to
relate to statements made by Mr Summers.

In any case, for Mr Summers, please feel free to use
these for your own welfare (benefit); untaxed and not
mandated by government. <g>
```

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>

```


"LX-i" <lxi0007@netscape.net> wrote in message 
news:fMGdnX8gR4BxiGbbnZ2dnUVZ_uOmnZ2d@comcast.com...
> docdwarf@panix.com wrote:
<snip>.
>
> Just because the government creates and distributes the "money" does not 
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
>
On the contrary, Daniel, every time a Government has tried to tinker with 
the free market the said Government has been severely burned. You cannot 
buck the market.

The example I remember best was when Nigel Lawson, Chancellor of the 
Exchequer, tried to prop the UK pound up by buying pounds with British 
reserves. He lost 70 Billion (and his job) in an afternoon and George Sorus 
reputedly made 3 billion for himself...


<snip>>
> Yes - but when I contract an hour of my life, complete with whatever 
> physical and mental skills I possess, to someone else for an agreed-upon 
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> whatever economic hocus-pocus they play on the employer.

Tell me about it :-)

However, that is the price we pay for living in a Democratic society and 
trying to ensure a reasonable standard of living for all. We can't be 
comfortable in cosy homes with food on the table, knowing there are beggars 
starving at the gates.

<anecdote follows... skip this if you don't like stories>

(I've been places where I've actually seen this... a palatial mansion with 
three Mercedes in the drive and security guards to keep the riff-raff out, 
with a young mother too weak to stand, propped up against the fence, while 
her pathetically undernourished two year old crawled towards a banana skin 
lying on the sidewalk. I've seen a man and his wife, living in a packing 
case shelter, with three kids, watch their child die from sickness and 
starvation and the anguish on their faces is etched in my memory. I've seen 
women and children beaten with a bamboo cane by a man on a truck, while they 
tried to pick up grains of rice, grain by grain, from the dust, where it had 
drizzled out of rice laden sacks on trucks taking it to a secure warehouse 
for sale on the black market. (The trucks had to wait while the armoured 
doors were rolled up. The women and kids knew there was rice to be had at 
this point and took the beating in order to get it.) These sacks were 
clearly marked as a present from the people of  the United States of America 
for distribution to the needy, but in a society without justice, graft and 
corruption rule. I've been in a crowded marketplace, felt someone tug on my 
sleeve and turned to see the face of Leprosy, the skin peeling off the 
bones, the body swathed in filthy bandages, the stumps of fingers as the 
remains of a hand are extended for charity, and looked into the eyes of 
what was once a person. A preventable and curable disease, when there are 
clinics financed from taxation.

What do you do?

I helped the mother and child with food and drink, but it was temporary. The 
parents in the packing case there was nothing I could do and I shall always 
feel sorry over that. The guy with the cane got a surprise. He suddenly 
found a 6 foot Kiwi with a demeanour that showed he was not happy, had 
joined him on the back of the truck, and was telling him calmly that if he 
didn't stop with the beating the cane would be inserted into one of his 
bodily orifices. Said cane was then broken into pieces and discarded. He 
stammered that they might mob the truck and my response was: "So what? The 
rice is supposed to be for them anyway." I made a promise to myself in that 
moment that I would never waste rice again, in memory of those people. To 
this day, if I have a rice dish in a Chinese restaurant and can't eat it 
all, I take it home and it is eaten. My friends think I'm "odd" but I have 
never told them the story. Besides, they think me odd in so many ways, it is 
just another... :-)

The leper I gave some money too, but I had no illusions it was pointless. 
The best I could do and have done was stop making jokes about lepers... 
(That doesn't mean YOU can't; you weren't there :-); it just means that I 
don't...) I still don't know if that was a man or a woman, but it was once 
Human and I shall always remember the eyes...

</end of anecdote>

The whole point of the above, Daniel, is that although we have far from 
perfect societies, I'd rather live in one where the Government wastes some 
of my tax than live in one where it is "every man for himself". At least I 
can make a noise and get others incensed about it and maybe even change the 
Government.  I guess that, as far as I'm concerned at least, the 
alternatives are far worse.

None of us are ever going to be happy about paying tax (unless of course, 
they brought in a fixed rate across the board and everybody paid the same, 
say 15%...), but we can rest assured that our doing so means people can be 
sheltered, fed, educated,and get basic medical care.

I realise that from your point of view you see your tax dollars being spent 
on things you don't approve of, and could not condone morally. That is 
particuarly hard. I don't approve of big desks and luxurious appointments 
for Civil Servants, but I'll swallow it as long as the schools and hospitals 
stay open :-)

>
> Your focus is on what's "legal" - mine is what's "right."  Those are often 
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
>>
There's nothing in any Democracy to stop us owning stuff. We just need to 
remember that not everyone is as "lucky" as we are. The able have a 
responsiblity to the less able, the strong have a responsibility to the 
weak. Sure, it gets screwed up by the lazy and the greedy, but by and large, 
it works. It is still better than the alternatives.

Pete.
```

```
> Tell me about it :-)

All right...  :)

[snip anecdote]

> say 15%...),

But *why* won't they do that?  It's the ultimate in fairness - you make 
$100, you cough up $15; you make $1,000, you cough up $150.  But the 
demagogues would rather give a free ride to some, and tax others more, 
because they've figured out that there are more on the low end than the 
high end, and they will vote their wallets instead of their morals.

> There's nothing in any Democracy to stop us owning stuff. We just need to 
> remember that not everyone is as "lucky" as we are. The able have a 
> responsiblity to the less able, the strong have a responsibility to the

Is it?  Who is to say that private charity could not do a better, more

And I don't believe it's luck at all.  "The rich get richer because they 
keep doing what it is that makes them rich - ditto for the poor." - Neal 
Boortz (US radio talk show host)  I started not so well off - we had 
very little growing up.  I remember getting my first clerical temp job 
for $7.50/hr - that was the highest per-hour wage rate that anyone in my 
family had ever earned!  I've changed what I've done, from things that 
didn't work to things that did.
```

- **References:** `<fMGdnX8gR4BxiGbbnZ2dnUVZ_uOmnZ2d@comcast.com> <5m1vncFb9grnU1@mid.individual.net> <T_2dnQVAxKXy6mHbnZ2dnUVZ_tqtnZ2d@comcast.com> <fdii7d$gpc$1@reader1.panix.com>`

```

<docdwarf@panix.com> wrote in message news:fdii7d$gpc$1@reader1.panix.com...
> In article <T_2dnQVAxKXy6mHbnZ2dnUVZ_tqtnZ2d@comcast.com>,
> LX-i  <lxi0007@netscape.net> wrote:
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> >> None of us are ever going to be happy about paying tax (unless of
course,
> >> they brought in a fixed rate across the board and everybody paid the
same,
> >> say 15%...),
> >
…[12 more quoted lines elided]…
> who has 85% of ten times that income.

I notice, Mr Dwarf, a slight, perhaps unintentional change
from omitting the term "rate". I agree that a "flat tax" is
regressive because all pay the same amount without
regard to income, thus the effective tax rate decreases with
increased income. A pure "flat-rate tax" is neither regressive
nor progressive because the effective rate remains the same
with increased income.

Advocates of "flat-rate tax" call their plans progressive
because their proposals include an exemption before the
rate is applied, thus the effective tax rate increases with
increased income.

Since I am unfamiliar with any "entirely different situation"
that could alter these truths, I may reasonably conclude
that those who would call a "flat-rate tax" regressive are
simply opposed to all such plans and would use
name-calling, rather than facts, to support their opposition.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: [OT] Iraq

_(reply depth: 11)_

- **From:** docdwarf@panix.com ()

```
In article <13fps4ekn12icb2@corp.supernews.com>,
Rick Smith <ricksmith@mfi.net> wrote:
>
><docdwarf@panix.com> wrote in message news:fdii7d$gpc$1@reader1.panix.com...
…[30 more quoted lines elided]…
>with increased income.

Perhaps the terminology has changed since I last looked at such things.  
As I recall, a flat tax (one which is applied uniformly across a 
population, regardless of income or ability to pay) is always represented 
as a particular percentage of income (in the abovegiven example, 15%); 
this percentage is the tax's rate (http://www.m-w.com, rate, noun, def'n 
3b: 'a charge, payment, or price fixed according to a ratio, scale, or 
standard')

If that is the case then to speak of a flat tax is to speak of a flat tax 
at a given rate; I am not sure how the term flat-rate tax (which you refer 
to surrounded by quotation marks, indicating... something) differs from 
flat tax (which, as noted above, is always at a given rate).

>
>Advocates of "flat-rate tax" call their plans progressive
>because their proposals include an exemption before the
>rate is applied, thus the effective tax rate increases with
>increased income.

If there are variations in its application then the rate is not, by 
definition (http://www.m-w.com, flat, adj, def'n 6b(1): not varying: FIXED 
<a flat rate>), flat.

>Since I am unfamiliar with any "entirely different situation"
>that could alter these truths, I may reasonably conclude
>that those who would call a "flat-rate tax" regressive are
>simply opposed to all such plans and would use
>name-calling, rather than facts, to support their opposition.

It may be, Mr Smith, that some see the word 'flat' and assume, perhaps 
incorrectly, that it is being used in a manner which reflects a definition 
found in a commonly-accepted source... and that others see that calling 
something which has variations in it 'flat' as valid.

DD
```

```
> >> 85% of one income is in an entirely different situation than someone

> >> who has 85% of ten times that income.

…[19 more quoted lines elided]…
> flat tax (which, as noted above, is always at a given rate).

Perhaps it differs in degree of precision--"flat-rate tax",
as a specific reference to a concept, being less ambiguous.
Since I am a stickler for precision, I dug into my closet
and pulled out the book that introduced me to the concept;
"Low Tax, Simple Tax, Flat Tax" by Robert E. Hall and
Alvin Rabushka, 1983. A quick thumbing-through of the
book showed that "flat tax", "flat-tax", and "flat-rate tax"
were regarded as synonymous, within the context of a tax
on income assessed at a flat rate. (All three variations
found on the same page! And there are other reasons to
beleive the book was rushed to press!)

> >
> >Advocates of "flat-rate tax" call their plans progressive
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> <a flat rate>), flat.

Page 21 of the book, last paragraph, "The flat tax can ....
Also, a flat-rate tax is progressive when a personal
allowance for adults and dependents is granted, ...."

While the statutory rate is flat (not varying, fixed); the
effective rate does vary by increasing with increased
income.

> >Since I am unfamiliar with any "entirely different situation"
> >that could alter these truths, I may reasonably conclude
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> something which has variations in it 'flat' as valid.

Or, that some may see or use the word "flat" and fail to
recognize when its use is ambiguous and clarity depends on
context. For the driver, a tax for driver's license renewal
may be flat, but for the state, a flat rate--the same rate or
tax for every driver in the state. For the taxpayer, property
taxes may be flat for non-ad valorem and flat rate for ad
valorem assessments; but, for the tax assessor, flat rate
for both.

In the context of the U.S. income tax, the tax is a flat rate
tax within ranges; but it is not a "flat-rate tax". The latter
term seems to remove all doubt.


Given the immediate context of "The flat tax has been
called a regressive tax" and my knowing that a "flat-rate
tax" can not be a regressive tax, my mind appears to have
jumped to examples of "flat tax" which are regressive.
And, with that jump, could not determine your intended
use of "flat tax".
```

```
Mr Summers original conditions, quoted above, were 'It's the ultimate in 
fairness - you make $100, you cough up $15; you make $1,000, you cough up 
$150.'; no allowances, deductions, protected investments or deferred 
interest... a rate of taxation that is 'not varying, FIXED' as per the 
definition I cited.

proposing that certain income(s) are exempt and others will be taxed at a 
rate not to exceed (n)%.

>effective rate does vary by increasing with increased

Exactly.  This reminds of me 'cash is fact, profit is opinion'... because

wind up with a tax system which approaches the current one.

…[5 more quoted lines elided]…

My situation appeared to be the reverse... I saw the word 'flat' and 
realised that the use being made of it seemed to be outside of the 
accepted definitions; this is sometimes called 'idiosyncratic use'.  
There's nothing wrong with such use, of course... but it helps to cut down 
confusion when it is made clear, up front, that the definitions found in 
commonly-accepted references no longer apply.

[snip]

>Given the immediate context of "The flat tax has been
>called a regressive tax" and my knowing that a "flat-rate
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>use of "flat tax".

Glad I was able to help clear that up by - completely unintentionally, 
mind you - sticking with the definitions (the ones equating flat tax and 
flat-rate tax') used in a book with which you were familiar.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ OT: Taxes

_(reply depth: 14)_

- **From:** Howard Brazee <howard@brazee.net>
- **Date:** 2007-10-01T11:17:27-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<0ga2g3hhm3bg8tr4vi4gj1e8vpu2tu9qsd@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<13fps4ekn12icb2@corp.supernews.com> <fditp5$hao$1@reader1.panix.com> <13fqj1reqtrpj5b@corp.supernews.com> <fdjtrj$3j7$1@reader1.panix.com>`

```
On Fri, 28 Sep 2007 22:05:40 +0000 (UTC), docdwarf@panix.com () wrote:

>Mr Summers original conditions, quoted above, were 'It's the ultimate in 
>fairness - you make $100, you cough up $15; you make $1,000, you cough up 
>$150.'; no allowances, deductions, protected investments or deferred 
>interest... a rate of taxation that is 'not varying, FIXED' as per the 
>definition I cited.

Mind you, nobody is suggesting that this is Gross income.   Therefore
there will be tax lawyers figuring out how to define expenses to the
benefit of those who run their own businesses.   The tax code would
still grow, with exemptions all over the place for various companies.

I'm more in favor of replacing income tax with sales tax.

What this does is:
1.   Rich people either invest their money or spend it.   Investments
are now tax breaks, so there's not change - spent money gets taxed.
2.   Most states currently don't tax food, drugs, & rent.    Poor
people spend virtually all of their money on such, so it's not
regressive.
3.   Sony America is set up to not have much income - by paying more
money from its products it buys from Sony Japan.   But taxing Sony
equipment is exactly the same as taxing Apple equipment.
4.   Tax code is much simpler.

We still have the issues of buying overseas - as we have now with
earning overseas.   But those are old issues with lots of implement
solutions.
```

```
In article <X7fMi.3056$4V6.2467@newssvr14.news.prodigy.net>, "Michael Mattias" <mmattias@talsystems.com> wrote:
>"Judson McClendon" <judmc@sunvaley0.com> wrote in message 
>news:w2bMi.3239$O9.1143@bignews6.bellsouth.net...
>> The original US constitution made it illegal to impose an income tax
>
>The original constitution did no such thing.

It certainly did:

"No Capitation, or other direct, Tax shall be laid, unless in Proportion to 
the Census or enumeration herein before directed to be taken."
[Article I, Section 9, paragraph 4]

Income tax is a direct tax, and it is not in proportion to the census -- and 
therefore illegal under the Constitution as originally adopted. That's why an 
amendment was required in order to allow income tax.

>However, the original constitution did NOT grant the government the right to 
>tax income; and what powers were not granted the government were
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>legality, that is.

How ironic.
>
>It never takes a law to limit the power of the federal government... it 
>takes a law to expand it. (e.g, Patriot Act, Constitutional Amendment).

No *law* can expand the power of the federal government. Only a Constitutional 
Amendment can do that. To the extent that the Patriot Act is constitutional, 
it does not increase the powers of the federal government; rather, it permits 
the federal government to exercise power that it already had.
```

```
docdwarf@panix.com wrote:
> In article <T_2dnQVAxKXy6mHbnZ2dnUVZ_tqtnZ2d@comcast.com>,
> LX-i  <lxi0007@netscape.net> wrote:
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
> electorate... or maybe it is something else.

Like them campaigning saying they would do it, then forgetting that part 
once they're elected?  Say it ain't so!  :)

>> It's the ultimate in fairness - you make 
>> $100, you cough up $15; you make $1,000, you cough up $150.
> 
> The flat tax has been called a regressive tax, Mr Summers... are you 
> familiar with the arguments that have been made for this?

I am - and it's not regressive; it's neither regressive nor progressive. 
  It's fair - and being fair to those who have achieved more in this 
life is something that most politicians can't stomach.

I also have never seen an argument that wasn't based on emotion - with 
facts and numbers, the flat tax rate is the way to go.

> Someone who has 
> 85% of one income is in an entirely different situation than someone else 
> who has 85% of ten times that income.

True - but isn't nearly everyone in a different situation?  What is 
unfair about a level playing field?  I will know that if I make (x), 
I'll take home (x - 15%); if I make (y), I'll take home (y - 15%).

And, while we're on the subject of taxes, why tax earnings?  Wouldn't it 
be smarter to tax expenditures?  Not from the politician's perspective. 
   Tax it when you earn it, tax it when you spend it, tax it when you 
invest it, then tax it before it goes to your kids.  It's ridiculous! 
And why is *that* much money needed?  Because the government is doing 
things it has absolutely no business doing.

Withholding tax is probably the worst thing that has happened to our tax 
system, as far as transparency - it masks exactly how much is being 
taken out.  Ask the average person how much they pay in taxes, and 
they'll say "Oh, I don't pay - I get some back!"  ...just because they 
had too much taken from their checks to begin with.  Change the system 
and make people write out tax checks every quarter, and it would take 
about 3.1 months for the revolution to begin.  :)  If people actually 
see their earnings leaving their checking account and go to the 
government, they would understand exactly what they're paying.

>> But the 
>> demagogues would rather give a free ride to some, and tax others more, 
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> equitable way to redistribute property?

An argument to authority from you?  :)  I'm not familiar with geometric 
taxes, but if it redistributes property from the haves to the have nots, 
it doesn't sound right to me...
```

```
docdwarf@panix.com wrote:

> 'it's fair, it's fair, it's fair'.

Well, not that Wikipedia is authoritative, but it pretty much summarizes 
the point regarding regressive, progressive, and proportional taxes.

http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Regressive_tax

Note the example of what it's calling the "flat tax" in the second 
paragraph.  If Jane earns $10 and John earns $5, a $1 tax hits John with 
a higher rate.  What it terms the "proportional tax" is what Rick went 
to great lengths to clarify - a flat tax *rate*, not a flat tax 
*amount*.  It seems you're wanting to say you're talking about rates, 
then hit it with the complaints of a fixed amount.

>> True - but isn't nearly everyone in a different situation?
> 
> If nearly everyone is in a different situation and the tax treats them all 
> identically then it appears that the tax does not treat them according to 
> their situation.  How is that fair?

It's empirically fair!  Look at the first definition from 
http://dictionary.reference.com/browse/fair - "free from bias, 
dishonesty, or injustice."  Progressive tax structures are biased based 
on income - they are not impartial.

>> What is 
>> unfair about a level playing field?  I will know that if I make (x), 
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> results might not be equivalent.

Exactly.  I work hard, get a raise, and increase my earnings from x to 
y.  Are you saying it makes one lick of sense at all that I should now 
earn (y - *20%*) as a thank you for working my tail off?  :)

>> And, while we're on the subject of taxes, why tax earnings?
> 
> I'd rather not change the subject at the moment; what is being dealt with 
> here is of sufficient weight, for the nonce.

OK - but my point was made.
```
