###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: [OT] Iraq

_(reply depth: 16)_

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2007-10-03T17:12:34+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<5mgj5jFcn9f7U1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<E4adnYYkUZXpPJzanZ2dnUVZ_oaonZ2d@comcast.com> <fdt3ia$ikg$1@reader1.panix.com> <5mep12Fd4il5U1@mid.individual.net> <fdtsd6$j15$1@reader1.panix.com>`

```


<docdwarf@panix.com> wrote in message news:fdtsd6$j15$1@reader1.panix.com...
> In article <5mep12Fd4il5U1@mid.individual.net>,
> Pete Dashwood <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
…[39 more quoted lines elided]…
>
Well, we won't argue your premise, 'It is unjust for a government to tax 
citizens into poverty'... even though there may be valid exceptions to it... 
let's run with it and see where it goes.

Hmmm...OK, I've considered it.

It seems to me the unjustness arises not from the tax rate, but from an 
employer who pays such a wage. Given that a worker is working in such a job, 
said worker is still contributing the same proportion of his/her income to 
the general good, as everyone else. That is patently fair. The fact that 
said worker may now be descending into poverty, might be an incentive for 
said worker to withhold labour, or get another, better paying job from a 
more enlightened employer. When the laws of supply and demand kick into the 
labour market, employers requiring such labour can become enlightened 
overnight...

I've been on a few picket lines in my time (usually in support of other 
workers at the same place (computer people have rarely had problems getting 
a decent living wage)). Almost invariably (certainly in the cases in which 
I've been personally involved) the end result has been a satisfactory 
increase in wages, and no noticeable pain to the organization.

I do not advocate strike as an immediate negotiation step, or as a "gun to 
the head". Well run companies have good labour relations and pay a fair 
wage.

<anecdote - skip this if you don't like stories>
(The miners strike in Britain in 1978 (?) is an exception that springs 
immediately to mind...

The miners were being paid something like 100 pounds a week (when most 
people were earning less than half of that), and they wanted 135. I remember 
thinking to myself, "Would I go down a coal mine and work 8 hours at the 
coal face for 135 quid?" As the answer was an emphatic "No", I gave them my 
support. This consisted of donating food to their families when you went to 
the Supermarket. and various expressions of moral support to the pickets in 
the streets. Eventually it was resolved and they got their increase. 
However, it sowed the seeds for an inevitable Armageddon between Government 
and Unions.

In 1984 the final showdown came.

They stopped providing coal for electricity generation, during a severe 
winter and elderly people started dying from hypothermia.

Electricity in London was limited to a few hours a day.

I was living in an all electric flat in London at the time :-) It even had 
electric underfloor heating. I remember having to get in the car and drive 
around London to get warm during the hours when supply was off. It was a 
pretty awful time for everyone.

The miners were amongst the highest paid workers in Britain at that time, 
but it wasn't about pay, it was about politics. Does the Government run the 
country, or do the Unions?

Initially they were pretty confident; no Government had ever failed to give 
in and there was much public support.

This was the first time that the Miners Union had ever been seriously taken 
on by Government. Some mining families had a terrible time, while Arthur 
Scargill was flying around the Midlands by helicopter popping in here and 
there to rally the troops. Initially, I supported their case, but I changed 
my mind when I saw people dying and when Scargill showed no sign of 
negotiating. (So did most of the population...)) The strike was illegal 
anyway, because Scargill had refused to put it to a ballot, as required by 
the Union rules. (Had he done so, there would have been no strike.)

They picked the wrong Prime Minister (Thatcher - Heath would have buckled)) 
and the wrong leader (Scargill, who sold them all out anyway in the end), 
so, instead of making their point, they simply lost all public support and 
that was the beginning of the end for the coal industry in GB - they could 
import it from Poland for a fraction of what it cost to mine at home.

</anecdote - skip this if you don't like stories>

I don't believe the tax rate in your given scenario causes unjustness, Doc. 
There is obvious injustice in an employer offering a derisory wage, and it 
is up to the market place to correct that.

Pete
```

```
In article <5mgj5jFcn9f7U1@mid.individual.net>,
Pete Dashwood <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
>
>
><docdwarf@panix.com> wrote in message news:fdtsd6$j15$1@reader1.panix.com...

[snip]

>> All righty... given that the 'generally accepted concept' of 'an unjust
>> situation' includes 'It is unjust for a government to tax citizens into
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
>employer who pays such a wage.

I did not offer to determine the source of the injustice, Mr Dashwood, but 
merely to point out that it exists.  This I believe I have done.

>Given that a worker is working in such a job, 
>said worker is still contributing the same proportion of his/her income to 
>the general good, as everyone else. That is patently fair.

Given that the same worker, by doing 'as everyone else', is being driven 
into poverty, it is demonstrably unjust.  Funny how things work out that 
way, eh?  Replace a tax-structure that allows for exemptions with one that 
is 'fair' and an injustice results that requires an adjustment in wage 
laws to compensate.

>The fact that 
>said worker may now be descending into poverty, might be an incentive for 
>said worker to withhold labour, or get another, better paying job from a 
>more enlightened employer.

The fact that said worked would be taxed into poverty is an injustice... 
so now one needs to find another employer or line of work in order to 
avoid injustice.  Curious system of government arising from that.

>When the laws of supply and demand kick into the 
>labour market, employers requiring such labour can become enlightened 
>overnight...

That they can... and raise the prevailing rate... or automate the jobs 
right out of existence or offshore them to another country.  It does not 
change the fact that the flat-rate tax, in that it drives low-end workers 
into poverty, is an act of injustice... which is what was required to be 
proved.

>
>I've been on a few picket lines in my time (usually in support of other 
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>increase in wages, and no noticeable pain to the organization.

I am of two minds about strikes, in that the only strikes I've seen have 
been the result of union actions.  On the one hand there have been 
marvelous gains made for the workforce made by such, despite the wails of 
'but it'll drive us out of business!'... on the other hand, unions are 
now, themselves, big businesses, with all associated benefits and vices.

[snip]

>I don't believe the tax rate in your given scenario causes unjustness, Doc. 
>There is obvious injustice in an employer offering a derisory wage, and it 
>is up to the market place to correct that.

How's this, Mr Dashwood?  Paying a wage is a matter of both parties' 
participating; one to offer, one to accept.

It is up to the employer to offer a wage as low as possible in order to 
get as much work as possible, just as it is up to the employee to demand a 
wage as high as possible in order to give as little work as possible.  As 
long as demands fall within the bounds of law the fact of an injustice - 
not an illegality but an injustice - remains.

DD

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: [OT] Iraq

_(reply depth: 17)_

- **From:** Howard Brazee <howard@brazee.net>
- **Date:** 2007-10-03T09:36:19-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<lad7g3hiqv0s3d7909o6qf81h1o9om3695@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<E4adnYYkUZXpPJzanZ2dnUVZ_oaonZ2d@comcast.com> <fdt3ia$ikg$1@reader1.panix.com> <5mep12Fd4il5U1@mid.individual.net> <fdtsd6$j15$1@reader1.panix.com> <5mgj5jFcn9f7U1@mid.individual.net>`

```
On Wed, 3 Oct 2007 17:12:34 +1300, "Pete Dashwood"
<dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:

>It seems to me the unjustness arises not from the tax rate, but from an 
>employer who pays such a wage. Given that a worker is working in such a job, 
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
>overnight...

That is an interesting POV in the "fair tax" argument.    And that is
to put "flat tax" on one side but to put "supply and demand" on the
other side.

The "supply and demand" side says "tax to the maximum revenue".   If
you tax enough that too much wealth leaves the tax base, then you tax
too much.    Lafler curve economics works.

And taxing can include mandating rules that make it expensive to hire
workers - or to make it expensive to work.    For instance, a law
requiring day-care workers to have teaching degrees can make day-care
so expensive that parents decide that it's not worth staying in the
work space.

Socialists often want to mandate "fair" rent with rent controls. Maybe
that isn't so different from mandating "fair" taxes with tax controls.
```

Ahhhhh... so if there are grounds for taxing citizens into poverty which

>As you made up the premise yourself, you cannot object to my amending it so, 
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>that is causing the injustice.

Leaving aside the trifling difficulty of 'the criteria for a reasonable 
tax rate'... in law, Mr Dashwood, cause is determined by the action of an 
agent, sometimes referred to as the 'but for' principle.  In the case 
above, a tax structure which does not collect from those earning less than 
(rate) so as not to drive them into poverty is replaced by a flat tax, 
which *does* drive them into poverty.

But for the levying of the new tax said persons would not be in poverty.

The government levies the tax, hence the government does the driving... 
but all that aside, if you recall my original quotation, from 
<http://groups.google.com/group/comp.lang.cobol/msg/92e13b02a57d5c2c?dmode=source>

--begin quoted text:

So, Mr Summers... if it can be shown that a flat tax in the sense you 
describe - level playing field and all that - is an act of injustice then 
your argument disintergrates, correct?  This is rather simple to 
demonstrate, you know.

--end quoted text

... you will notice that the agent of injustice is not specified, just 
that it is caused.

DD

America's "poverty" class, I do not see a flat tax rate as injust.

Mr Summers, if you are saying that government-established taxes are to be 
considered as identical to market-determined priced then you'd have no

your support of market-forces rather than government fiat.

Mr Summers, that is similar to being opposed to the death penalty because 
people will die, anyhow... but if you disagree with:

'It is an act of injustice for a government to tax citizens into poverty'

... then the most ready conclusion is that it is not an act of injustice 
for this to happen.  If it is not an act of injustice then it is either 
neutral, and a matter of opinion, or it is an act of justice.

If it is an act of justice for a government to tax citizens into poverty 
then supporting a flat tax, which you believe would *discourage* this from 
happening, shows that you oppose a government acting justly.  That being 
the case, there is no wonder that we disagree.

I'm not saying that the government should set prices; rather, that 
*anything* can be considered "more of a burden" to those whose incomes 
are in the lower percentiles.

If person A makes $100/wk, milk costs $5/gallon, and consumes 2 gallons 
per week, 10% of his income goes toward milk.  If person B makes $1,000 
a week, should he pay $50/gallon for milk?  Where's the incentive to 
earn more money, if the benefits it brings are negated?

> If you don't like the sources you supply then perhaps you might find 
> others.

My biggest problem is that people are all different - different choices, 
different priorities, different desires.  Instead of just *establishing* 
a level playing field, and realize that as in any game, there will be 
winners and losers, we're trying to slant the field so the *outcome* is 
level.  That's not the way life works in any other arena - why try to 
apply it to something as critical as funding the government?

>>> As noted earlier: 
>>>

people are going to die anyway.

absolutes.  :)  Isn't it you who has repeated posted "Nothing is 
absolute, including this statement"?

I believe it is the responsibility of *every* citizen to do their 
equitable part to fund the government.  Whether they are below, near the 
margins, or well above some government-established poverty line does not 
change that.  The act of the government collecting this is completely just.

> If it is not an act of injustice then it is either

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: [OT] Iraq

_(reply depth: 20)_

- **From:** LX-i <lxi0007@netscape.net>
- **Date:** 2007-10-14T16:08:41-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<l6udnafkCcEPDo_anZ2dnUVZ_tKinZ2d@comcast.com>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<fep5ks$ado$1@reader1.panix.com>`
- **References:** `<tb-dnbK1uvJux5nanZ2dnUVZ_vmlnZ2d@comcast.com> <fe2boh$9on$1@reader1.panix.com> <QOmdnWIotKVrc5ranZ2dnUVZ_sGvnZ2d@comcast.com> <fep5ks$ado$1@reader1.panix.com>`

```
docdwarf@panix.com wrote:
> In article <QOmdnWIotKVrc5ranZ2dnUVZ_sGvnZ2d@comcast.com>,
> LX-i  <lxi0007@netscape.net> wrote:
…[35 more quoted lines elided]…
> that thing, or something else, entire?

I'm saying that the definition provided for "regressive" taxes can apply 
to absolutely anything.  "More of a burden" is also subjective.  I've 
already established that I do *not* believe a flat tax amount is fair, 
while a fixed tax rate is.  Who is to say that the $10,000-earner's 
$1,500 is "more of a burden" than the $100,000-earner's $15,000?  I do 
*not* believe the government should be in the position to be trying to 
make these sorts of judgment calls.

I furthermore believe that the labeling of graduated income tax as 
"progressive", while labeling things like sales taxes as "regressive", 
is deceptive.  When people's earnings are reduced before they see them, 
they don't realize what they're paying.  When you pick something up off 
the shelf and take it to the check-out counter, you see the jump between 
the asking price and the amount of money you have to part with to take 
it out of the store.  (Of course, I shouldn't be surprised - the folks 
who label themselves "progressives" don't want the general public to 
know exactly what they're doing either.)

I'm not worried about poverty lines - a somewhat fixed percentage of the 
American public will *always* be below that line, as it's moved when too 
many people move out of it.  Yet another deceptive practice that 
politicians use to foment wealth envy, and let the two wolves and the 
sheep vote on what's for dinner.
```

- **References:** `<tb-dnbK1uvJux5nanZ2dnUVZ_vmlnZ2d@comcast.com> <fe2boh$9on$1@reader1.panix.com> <QOmdnWIotKVrc5ranZ2dnUVZ_sGvnZ2d@comcast.com> <fep5ks$ado$1@reader1.panix.com> <l6udnafkCcEPDo_anZ2dnUVZ_tKinZ2d@comcast.com> <kjn9h3h6c1ro2t24110o72i6f22r3lps53@4ax.com> <wP2dnYqwtv_v84janZ2dnUVZ_uKpnZ2d@comcast.com>`

```
On Tue, 16 Oct 2007 20:06:50 -0600, LX-i <lxi0007@netscape.net> wrote:

>Fair is pretty easily defined...
>
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
>achieve income.

EVERY tax punishes those that have something to be taxed.    (And any
income tax "punishes" those who achieve income).

Impartial doesn't mean that the results are the same to everybody, it
means that the rules are known and followed without bias.    Your
definition is like complaining that it isn't fair that association
football (soccer) goalies can use their hands and forwards can't.

Unfair taxes are those that give advantages to those with the best tax
lawyers or best lobbyists.    Fair taxes are those that follow simple
rules that everybody knows about ahead of time and which are
implemented consistently.

Your definitions of "Fair" (which I agree with) do not support your
contention that progressive taxes are less fair than flat taxes.

Progressive tax schemes are as impartial as any other taxes.
```

```
On Tue, 02 Oct 2007 18:53:44 -0600, LX-i <lxi0007@netscape.net> wrote:

>What was your problem again with a flat tax rate?

It is impossible to define income in a way that permits "no exceptions".

1.  PF, a potato farmer, grows one potato at a cost of .90 and sells it to Potato Chip
Manufacturer (PFM) for 1.00. How much is his tax on 'income' of 1.00?

2. PF signs a contract with PFM agreeing to plant their potato breed and grow it according
to their specs. His cost is 1.00 and he sells the potato for 1.20. How much is his tax?

3. PCM hires PF as a contractor, agrees to pay his 1.00 expenses plus a salary of .20. How
much of his 1.20 'income' is taxable?

4. PF outsources the farming to India, which delivers potatoes for .50. He still receives
1.00 for expenses plus .20 for salary. How much of that 1.20 is taxable?

5. PF spends the growing season in the Waldorf-Astoria of Mumbai, where the rent is .50.
How much of his 1.20 is taxable? If different from 4, explain why a flat rate tax should
subsidize the Waldorf of Mumbai.
```

Is it? I think that rather depends on your will to do so.

>
> 1.  PF, a potato farmer, grows one potato at a cost of .90 and sells it to 
> Potato Chip
> Manufacturer (PFM) for 1.00. How much is his tax on 'income' of 1.00?

15%

>
> 2. PF signs a contract with PFM agreeing to plant their potato breed and 
> grow it according
> to their specs. His cost is 1.00 and he sells the potato for 1.20. How 
> much is his tax?

15%
>
> 3. PCM hires PF as a contractor, agrees to pay his 1.00 expenses plus a 
> salary of .20. How
> much of his 1.20 'income' is taxable?

15%
>
> 4. PF outsources the farming to India, which delivers potatoes for .50. He 
> still receives
> 1.00 for expenses plus .20 for salary. How much of that 1.20 is taxable?

15%

>
> 5. PF spends the growing season in the Waldorf-Astoria of Mumbai, where 
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>

15%

It is a very good example Robert and I have much sympathy with it. But I'm 
still not persuaded.

The fact is that income can be misleading and people can have good and bad 
years. :-) It is also a bit contrived to use a commercial example when we 
are talking primarily about personal tax. I would see a flat tax rate on 
PERSONAL INCOME
 (earned and unearned) and a flat tax on CORPORATE PROFIT.

Nevertheless, your example highlights some of the problems. Even personal 
income may not meet or exceed personal expenditure and there is an argument 
for asking why people should be worse off than Companies. As individuals, we 
are expected to curb our expenditure to meet our income (including paying 
tax) or we must set up the necessary debt instruments.

 As we are never going to see it in practice (and I am currently enjoying 
being debt free :-)), I guess I can afford to be blase about it... :-)

Pete.

On Wed, 3 Oct 2007 18:13:59 +1300, "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
wrote:

>
>
…[54 more quoted lines elided]…
> (earned and unearned) and a flat tax on CORPORATE PROFIT.

Income tax reform has an oversimplified premise: income = paycheck. When asked to deal
with income other than paychecks, all they can say is out of scope, tax it like a
business. 

There is a HUGE difference between income and business tax. Income tax is on gross
receipts; business tax is on net profit. A typical employee might pay 15% of 60,000 =
9,000. If he were a business, he's pay 20% of 2,000 (increase in net worth) = 400. 

If the reform passed, every worker with a three digit IQ would become a business, by
switching from employee to contractor. 

Exempting non-employee income gives lie to the claim "no exceptions".

>Nevertheless, your example highlights some of the problems. Even personal 
>income may not meet or exceed personal expenditure and there is an argument 
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
>being debt free :-)), I guess I can afford to be blase about it... :-)

US contractors (who do it right) can legally receive $70K in tax-free expense
reimbursement -- no income tax, no FICA, no state tax, nada. The income they receive above
that puts us into a very low tax bracket.

Just in case no-one else has mentioned it: the tax situation is not
that simple in the UK. To avoid tax on earnings in one year one must
be outside of the UK for in excess of one year. That means as much as
18 months to avoid tax on 6 months earnings. However, as a foreign
national resident in the UK, earning oodles of dosh overseas, it is
possible to reduce one's UK tax burden to the min imum by agreeing
with the IR a nominal tax payment (�25k?).

On Thu, 4 Oct 2007 08:04:23 -0500, "HeyBub" <heybubNOSPAM@gmail.com>
wrote:

>Robert wrote:
>>>
…[28 more quoted lines elided]…
>
Having worked as an expatriate for over 5 years some time ago, I have
experience in this area.  Depending on the country you are living in
and what reciprocal tax agreements they have with the US, you can
still end up paying income tax albeit to your host country.  And, at
least in the countries I lived in, their tax rate was higher than the
28% bracket I was in at the time.

There was the exclusion as noted but it was easy to have income above
that amount.  This is because they count your paycheck and any
allowances you get for being an expat.  In my case I got a travel
allowance, a goods and services allowance and my rent was paid.  All
of that added to my income.  But my foreign tax bill was an expense I
could deduct.  All in all, I don't recall ever owing any income tax
while I was an expat but never got a refund either which was alright
since I didn't have and federal or state taxes being withheld.

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: [OT] Iraq

_(reply depth: 10)_

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2007-09-29T16:21:36+12:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<5m626iFbr0lgU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<zNGdnb-Q8paiZGjbnZ2dnUVZ_uKpnZ2d@comcast.com> <fd5mc6$1b6$1@reader1.panix.com> <SbadnSDaL696DmvbnZ2dnUVZ_qiinZ2d@comcast.com> <fd71j7$5te$1@reader1.panix.com> <fMGdnX8gR4BxiGbbnZ2dnUVZ_uOmnZ2d@comcast.com> <5m1vncFb9grnU1@mid.individual.net> <T_2dnQVAxKXy6mHbnZ2dnUVZ_tqtnZ2d@comcast.com> <13fq72mgq86ee06@corp.supernews.com>`

```


"Charles Hottel" <chottel@earthlink.net> wrote in message 
news:13fq72mgq86ee06@corp.supernews.com...
>
> "LX-i" <lxi0007@netscape.net> wrote in message 
…[19 more quoted lines elided]…
>
I don't think so.

Many companies (and individuals) pay high fees to specialists to avoid 
paying tax. If the rate was reasonable (say, 15%) there would be no need to 
avoid tax, and it wouldn't be possible anyway. (No exemptions, expenses, 
thresholds, etc.)

If you are right, and there actually was a shortfall, it could be made up by 
raising the percentage on invisibles and corporate profit by a couple of 
points. For example, companies under the new regime might pay 20% on profit 
instead of the flat 15%. It is still way better than the 30% they currently 
pay (or actually DON'T pay, because they use eleaborate schemes to avoid 
showing profit.)

There are other, less tangible benefits from such a system also. For 
example, everyone has equal "value" in terms of their contribution to 
society. You might therefore be less likely to hear pompous asses 
complaining about how their personal taxes were supporting a small hospital 
and they therefore are of much more importance than everybody else, and such 
nonsense.

Even if the revenue raised WAS less than currently, bureaucrats would have 
to learn to adapt to the available funds; most of them would benefit from 
"living on a budget" as many mere mortals have to.

It would go hand-in-hand with a drive to reduce corruption and sandbagging; 
no more $200 toilet seats for the military, and such..., much more openness 
and accontability in local and national government, and so on...

I don't think it will happen, but it would if I ruled the World :-)

(Be thankful I don't... :-))

Pete.
```

Don't get me wrong.  I like the idea of a flat tax of some kind or a 
variation thereof. I think a creative way could be found to make it work, 
but I haven't thought about the details very much.  That being said, 
however, I just did a rough calculation of your above suggested 15% and I 
believe that at 15% I would end up paying more taxes.  Right now I have 
approximately 14% to 15% of my gross adjusted income deducted in taxes even 
though my supposed tax bracket is I think 33%. This is because I claim extra 
exemptions.  Even with that because I can benefit from itemizing deductions, 
I usually receieve some (<$1000) refund. So I am paying less than 15% now. 
People with incomes that place them in lower tax brackets may be renting 
because they cannot afford to buy a house and therefore they may not benefit 
from itemizing deductions. I believe they will end up paying more also. The 
analysis that I have read (a long time ago) indicated that 15% would not be 
high enough.

Hres is a link to current tax brackets in the USA: 
http://www.moneychimp.com/features/tax_brackets.htm
```

```
In article <5m626iFbr0lgU1@mid.individual.net>,
Pete Dashwood <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:

[snip]

>It would go hand-in-hand with a drive to reduce corruption and sandbagging; 
>no more $200 toilet seats for the military, and such..., much more openness 
>and accontability in local and national government, and so on...

Mr Dashwood, this one is difficult.  Those who profit from the armed 
forces and military conflicts have existed for... well, about as long as 
armed forces and military conflicts have.  (a comic-strip character in the 
United States of America, Little Orphan Annie, had a benefactor who made 
his pile supplying the military... 'Daddy' Warbucks)

Given that there is, in the words of Country Joe Mcdonald, 'plenty good 
money to be made supplying the Army with the tools of its trade' there's 
plenty good room for fraud, as well... and while a fraudulently-made pair 
of pliers can be a nuisance around the house the same flaw, when 
encountered in combat during some ad-hoc equipment repairs, can be a bit 
more lethal for a few more people.

More specialised equipment, more specifications for equipment, more 
inspectors to insure that equipment is up to specs, more managers to 
co-ordinate the inspectors... these are all costs which need to be figured 
into the price of the product.

And, of course, as with any system... humans make it and humans can 
game/break it.

DD
```

- **References:** `<zNGdnb-Q8paiZGjbnZ2dnUVZ_uKpnZ2d@comcast.com> <fd5mc6$1b6$1@reader1.panix.com> <SbadnSDaL696DmvbnZ2dnUVZ_qiinZ2d@comcast.com> <fd71j7$5te$1@reader1.panix.com> <fMGdnX8gR4BxiGbbnZ2dnUVZ_uOmnZ2d@comcast.com> <5m1vncFb9grnU1@mid.individual.net> <T_2dnQVAxKXy6mHbnZ2dnUVZ_tqtnZ2d@comcast.com> <13fq72mgq86ee06@corp.supernews.com> <5m626iFbr0lgU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
Pete Dashwood wrote:
> It would go hand-in-hand with a drive to reduce corruption and sandbagging; 
> no more $200 toilet seats for the military, and such..., much more openness 
> and accontability in local and national government, and so on...

You know, that brings up an interesting point about the way budgeting 
works in the military.  If an organization does not spend its entire 
budget one year, it loses that amount in the next years' budget 
automatically.  The fiscal year ends 30 Sep, so we just went through 
that.  It's a juggling act - if you go a year without upgrading much 
infrastructure, you can be screwed the next year by not having the funds 
to do so.  This has led to an end-of-the-year buying spree, and $200 
toilet seats run through that quickly.

I'm not saying it's right, I'm just explaining the culture in which that 
could happen without too many red flags going up.

(We benefited this year from not only our money - the development team 
is getting new computers and wide-screen monitors, which will really 
help productivity - but other folks' money as well.  Our budgeting 
people had contracts ready to execute, and when other orgs couldn't 
spend their money, they transferred it to us.  Good times at the Safety 
Center!)
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: [OT] Iraq

_(reply depth: 10)_

- **From:** "HeyBub" <heybubNOSPAM@gmail.com>
- **Date:** 2007-09-29T06:27:22-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<13fsdj9d8td00a6@news.supernews.com>`
- **References:** `<zNGdnb-Q8paiZGjbnZ2dnUVZ_uKpnZ2d@comcast.com> <fd5mc6$1b6$1@reader1.panix.com> <SbadnSDaL696DmvbnZ2dnUVZ_qiinZ2d@comcast.com> <fd71j7$5te$1@reader1.panix.com> <fMGdnX8gR4BxiGbbnZ2dnUVZ_uOmnZ2d@comcast.com> <5m1vncFb9grnU1@mid.individual.net> <T_2dnQVAxKXy6mHbnZ2dnUVZ_tqtnZ2d@comcast.com> <13fq72mgq86ee06@corp.supernews.com>`

```
Charles Hottel wrote:
>>
>> But *why* won't they do that?  It's the ultimate in fairness - you
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> accept.

That's the flat tax. I propose the flat-flat tax. Take the budget (say $1 
trillion) divide by the number of people (say 250 million). That means that 
every person has to kick in $4,000. There are problems with this plan.

Consider a poor person who doesn't HAVE $4k. They could pay in kind - say 
one unit of blood platlets each month (at $400) and, in ten months, have 
their tax bill for the year paid.

But, but, but, what about the mother of four children all under the age of 
five! She can't give FIVE units of blood platlets a month and we certainly 
are not going to take blood from children!

She could give a kidney.

An artificial kidney machine costs, oh, say $45,000. One kidney would equate 
to about two years worth of taxes for her and her brood. Well enough, you 
may say, but what happens after two years? We're certainly not going to take 
the OTHER kidney!

She could donate a cornea.

Another two years. Before long her oldest child will be responsible for her 
own taxes (say at age 12) and mom can look forward to an early retirement.

Oh, sure, there are problems to be worked out, values assigned, "donation" 
clinics built, but nothing insurmountable and certainly nothing as 
complicated as the current tax scheme.

The basic objection to the flat-flat tax scheme, it seems to me, is that the 
burden falls most heavily on the lower rungs of society. Well, they're the 
ones who use the most government services! Sure, even the rich ride on 
roads, get mail, and benefit from military defense, so they should pay 
something. But you'll have to admit that the poor make use of the public 
schools, hospitals, and jails more than the affluent.

Seems fair to me that people should pay for what they get.
```

On Mon, 1 Oct 2007 15:16:53 -0500, "HeyBub" <heybubNOSPAM@gmail.com>
wrote:

>Satire? SATIRE?
>
>You may be too young to remember when McGovern pledged to give every 
>American $1000. I sent him a note suggesting the above scheme, except back 
>then it was a tax of $1000. Never got a reply.

Not a lot of CoBOL programmers who are that young anymore.

I remember when Nixon basically proposed the same thing.   

The funny thing is that I don't think his scheme was that far out, if
done right (which would be unlikely).

I'd like to see our standard personal deduction replaced by something
like that.   A "welfare" plan that applies to everybody without having
to qualify by showing that he can't work has its advantages.  Make it
cheaper to deduct it because that's cheaper for the taxpayer.

But I'd rather see a kid growing up thinking his mother is a waitress
because she got a job on Friday and Saturday nights - than to think
the mother he loves was a able to scam the government into welfare.

The thing is, it needs to *replace* current federal welfare supports,
not add to it - and it should not come with the kinds of bureaucratic
control that the state dearly loves.

Howard Brazee wrote:
> On Mon, 1 Oct 2007 15:16:53 -0500, "HeyBub" <heybubNOSPAM@gmail.com>
> wrote:
…[25 more quoted lines elided]…
> control that the state dearly loves.

Welfare rolls have been going down. Especially in New Orleans.

The thing is, we're on our way to a flat tax. It's called the AMT - 
Alternative Minimum Tax and, because it was not indexed for inflation, more 
and more taxpayers are getting snagged.

Originally designed to "catch" the wealthy who used "loopholes" to avoid 
taxes, the AMT "caught" 19,000 in 1970.

This year, the AMT is expected to snare 27 million taxpayers.

There are those who suggest we just leave the AMT alone and eventually 
everybody will come under its provisions. We can then junk the original tax 
laws.

Sounds like a plan.

```
"LX-i" <lxi0007@netscape.net> wrote:
>
> But the idea that the "government is the solution" in the first place is what boggles my mind.  What has the government done well? 
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> them to do it!

You nailed it there. Not only has government always been lousy at
doing things, but even when it is currently in progress demonstrating
how lousy it is, people *still* want to throw more money at it. The
U.S. spends several times as much money per student as any other
country, but has the worst secondary educational system of any
industrialized nation, worse than some third world countries. And
the answer is to give them more money? Insanity!

I know what would correct the U.S. educational system, and believe
it would do it in three years. First calculate the amount of money the
government spends per student, then offer *every* parent the option
of sending their child to public school as now, or getting a voucher
for 1/2 the amount the government spends per student to be used at
*any* private school the parent chooses. I sent my kids to private
schools, and any private school I know of would be ecstatic to get
1/2 what the government pays per student. The flight from public
schools would become a stampede, private schools would bloom.
Teachers would be leaving the public schools in droves, being
snapped up by private schools. For a couple of years this would be
somewhat chaotic, but I believe it would settle out in about three
years. Eventually deadwood teachers would be fired by the much
more efficient private sector, leaving only the better ones. Every child,
even poor ones, would benefit from a private school education, and
the taxpayers would be saved 1/2 the cost. Students would no longer
be indoctrinated in the current PC doctrine, but be educated in the
way the parents wanted it done. Win-Win-Win. The only losers
would be those vested in a failed public education system. They
betrayed our confidence, wasted our money and trashed our
children's educations to serve their own agenda. They rightfully
deserve prison sentences for malfeasance. Losing their jobs is far
less penalty than they deserve, and mild compared to damaging the
educational opportunities for millions of children.
```

```
Judson McClendon wrote:

>  The only losers
> would be those vested in a failed public education system. They
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> educational opportunities for millions of children.

Truely, the inmates are in charge of the asylum. Do you realize that a 
doctorate in education is the only terminal degree (aside from specialties 
like medicine or law) that does not require knowledge of a foreign language?

Several years ago, I did a bit of research. In my state - and probably 
yours - the following people are not qualified by law to teach in the pubic 
schools:

No living Nobel Laureate
No living Pulitzer Prize winner
No winner of Edgar, Newberry, or other prestigious literary prize
No sitting member of the federal judiciary
No living winner of the Fields Medal
No sitting member of the United States Senate
No living president or ex-president
No member of the Board of Directors of the Federal Reserve
etc.

They all lack the requisite "education" courses.

Does anyone doubt a retired nurse could teach high-school biology off the 
top of her head? Could a retired architect or civil engineer teach geometry 
or algebra or high-school physics? How hard would it be for a PhD in 
Chemistry to instruct in high-school?

As for the required "education" courses, remember a PhD in any science has 
twenty-years of classroom experience, plus, in most cases, one or two years 
of teaching undergraduate courses. How much MORE experience is required?

Bah!
```

- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<Il%Ki.57$pX5.46@newsfe14.lga>`
- **References:** `<VLydnRiEfowsIWzbnZ2dnUVZ_saknZ2d@comcast.com> <ik55f3tfr5h3i6ui4rtb2atc3rtghpqsio@4ax.com> <uJ-dnaJYC-eXqm7bnZ2dnUVZ_uevnZ2d@comcast.com> <fd0cpt$s7c$1@reader1.panix.com> <s8GdnRfyUvDoOWnbnZ2dnUVZ_g-dnZ2d@comcast.com> <kgkff3977i9faffo39r1b0682u0u48pas3@4ax.com> <2ZSdnWoW6pdAimbbnZ2dnUVZ_qGknZ2d@comcast.com> <zAMKi.132$z7.35@bignews7.bellsouth.net> <K3RKi.63304$hP1.26210@newsfe13.lga> <vzTKi.80824$Lu.54221@bignews8.bellsouth.net>`

```

Judson McClendon <judmc@sunvaley0.com> wrote in message
news:vzTKi.80824$Lu.54221@bignews8.bellsouth.net...
>
>
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
>

What >>I<< was trying to point out is that private enterprise is just as
ridden with dishonesty and incompetence as is the public system.

Nor do I have any faith in market forces.  "Market forces" are actually
individual people making individual choices, not some sentient and potent
entity (I call this the fallacy of thinking with collective nouns).
Individual people as a rule don't act effectively as players in unrestricted
free enterprise circumstances: they have miniscule clout as buyers compared
with the collective power of the sellers. Unrestricted free enterprise
ALWAYS leads to monopolies (or a small group of sellers acting together as a
monopoly).  The first case turns up where different products are available:
think computers, operating systems; where they're all selling the same thing
you get a group - insurance, banks, oil companies.  I'm not aware of any
product or service for which the number of competitors in actually
increasing.

And that's the ultimate failing point of your argument.  A monopoly or
cartel providing education will set the standards and the costs leaving the
consumer no choice.  Better to have a public monopoly: then at least we know
the head office address in Washington DC (or Ottawa, Ontario) instead of the
Bahamas, or Lichtenstein, etc.

PL
```

"LX-i" <lxi0007@netscape.net> wrote in message
news:bsSdnR8zmIah72HbnZ2dnUVZ_r7inZ2d@comcast.com...
> Rick Smith wrote:
> > "LX-i" <lxi0007@netscape.net> wrote in message

> > [snip]
> >> But the idea that the "government is the solution" in the first place
is
> >> what boggles my mind.
> >
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
> Well, sure, take the sentence out of context...  :)

Actually, I was adding an omitted, fundamental context,
that of rights. That "to secure rights" "government is the
solution" is fundamental to any discussion on welfarism,
in the U.S. Or, generally, if X is a right, then government
is the solution to secure that right.

>  Compare
> expenditures per student with average test scores, and even a Democrat
> should be able to see that private education is a much better investment
> than public education.

This is irrelevant to the fundamental question: Is there
a right to education? If the answer is no, then government
should have no direct involvement in education, though
it may be indirectly involved through its authority to
regulate commerce, such as, to prevent fraud. If the
answer is yes, then government involvement in education
is the solution to securing a right to education and
expenditures and test scores are merely complaints
about the particular choices made by some governments.

> >>  What has the government done well?
> >
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> Apart from law enforcement and defense, can you think of anything else?

Again, from my experience, most government regulated
or supplied public services that relate to infrastructure:
power, phone, water and sewer, trash collection, roads,
mail, emergency services, etc., seem to function well.

The part that extends into welfarism is where most of my
complaints arise, though I have no specific complaints
regarding public education, perhaps because I had no
children and, as a result of Florida's homestead exemption,
I do not pay any of the ad valorem taxes that are used to
fund public schools, locally. <g>
```

```
Rick Smith wrote:
> "LX-i" <lxi0007@netscape.net> wrote in message
> news:bsSdnR8zmIah72HbnZ2dnUVZ_r7inZ2d@comcast.com...
…[20 more quoted lines elided]…
> is the solution to secure that right.

True - but, the definition of "right" has been greatly expanded.  Just 
because something is nice and improves one's quality of life does not 
make it a right.  Is there a "right" to health care, to education, to 
Internet access?  There are politicians in this country who feel that 
there is.  There are others of us that feel that they are full of crap.  :)

(Sorry for missing your point above...)

>>  Compare
>> expenditures per student with average test scores, and even a Democrat
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
> about the particular choices made by some governments.

Well, I'd fall squarely in the "it's not a right" camp.  It's good to 
have, and it certainly makes life better, but it is *not* a right. 
Inherent human rights exist without taking away the rights of others.  I 
can be allowed to live without taking away the lives of others; I can 
hold my property without depriving others of holding theirs; I can 
decide where I go without restricting the mobility or freedom of others. 
  Government provides law enforcement to preserve these rights, and 
defense to ensure that another sovereign nation does not come and take 
these rights away.

However, I cannot get free health care, though, without someone else 
giving up the funding for it.  I cannot get free education without 
someone else giving up the funding for it.  Internet access is not free 
either - it takes funding to set up the infrastructure and provide the 
service to it.

In my opinion (which seems to be the same as the framers of our 
Constitution), this is the difference between a *right* and "nice-to-have."

>>>>  What has the government done well?
>>> Off-hand, prevention of anarchy comes to mind; but that
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
> mail, emergency services, etc., seem to function well.

True - but all these services have improved with deregulation and 
competition.

> The part that extends into welfarism is where most of my
> complaints arise, though I have no specific complaints
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> fund public schools, locally. <g>

Well, good for you.  :)  I brought up education as an example, but there 
are plenty others where government is mucking up what they shouldn't 
have gotten into in the first place.  Why do we fund "the arts" - isn't 
the "starving" part of the "starving artist" where they get their 
inspiration?

(heh heh...  I'm hear about it now...)

"I can be allowed to live without taking away the lives of others; I can

```
On Thu, 27 Sep 2007 21:17:15 -0600, LX-i <lxi0007@netscape.net> wrote:

>Rick Smith wrote:
>> "LX-i" <lxi0007@netscape.net> wrote in message
…[16 more quoted lines elided]…
>than public education.

Tell that to Milwaukee taxpayers, who pay MORE per student for the 25% attending private
schools under voucher than they pay for the 75% in the public system.

Total per student expenditures for public schools are about twice that of private schools.
Public schools spend 50% of their budget on administration whereas private schools spend
only 25%. Taxpayers should be clamoring for MORE administrators in both systems, because
it would save them money. Here's why --

When public schools account for administrators, they use something called Fund Based
Accounting, which makes each Federal program a little profit center.  If the program
doesn't bring in more money than it costs, the program and its administrator get the axe.
It's little different from a private business, except the revenue source is Federal
government rather than consumers or local taxpayers. 

The Feds are paying more than half of Milwaukee's public school budget. Private schools
have much fewer opportunities to feed at the Federal trough. If public schools fired half
its administrators, putting it on par with the private system, Federal subsidies would
disappear, causing property taxes to go up about 50%. 

Don't blame the Milwaukee public school system for the seeming inefficiency, blame
Washington. Is it inefficient? Students in both systems get about the same dollar amount
in instruction (teachers' salaries). Test scores are about the same for both. There's no
evidence that private schools are cheaper or better than public. 

There is healthy competition between public and private at the university level. Neither
is a clear winner over the other. Both public and private spend  the same $20,000 per
student (vs. $8,000 for high school).  Contrary to popular belief, universities' greatest
source of funds is not the state (for public) nor tuition (for private), nor endowments.
Most of the money comes from research grants and patent royalties. For instance, Uiniv. of
Virginia, a public school, receives only 8% of its revenues from the state and 15% from
tuition and fees. It it funded 43% by patents and 16% by research.
```
